1.2.1.4.1.1.8.9.1. Pro: To expand - 
[www.bioone.org](http://www.bioone.org/doi/full/10.2111/08-225.1)

[books.google.com](https://books.google.com/books?hl=en&lr=&id=MMggCAAAQBAJ&oi=fnd&pg=PP1&dq=grazing+and+carbon+sequestration&ots=VfxPUEmoF5&sig=DZYi5Ap6S-zQZJp7sCWCicyr0f4#v=onepage&q=grazing%20and%20carbon%20sequestration&f=false)

One problem here is that industrial ag dominates food production around the world. Therefore, it is a paradigm which also dominates much of the research. But, as permaculture, rotational grazing, and strategic agroecology management picks up steam, some research \(and a lot of antidotal experience\) is pointing to big benefits to this style of farming, including better carbon sequestering due to healthier soil.
1.2.1.4.1.1.8.9.2. Con: How the animals are raised does not affect the conversion ratio of the calories of plants they consume to the calories of meat they provide. Using farmed animals as a food source is inherently inefficient.
1.2.1.4.1.1.8.9.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.1.4.1.1.4.5.
1.2.1.4.1.1.8.9.2.2. Pro: -> See 1.2.1.4.1.1.4.6.
1.2.1.4.1.1.8.9.2.3. Con: How the animals are raised does not affect the conversion ratio of organisms on different trophic levels, but it does have an impact on the overall efficiency on a farm. The claim that animals == inefficiency is a zero-sum outlook on farm production, which only holds true for certain kinds of agriculture. In systems-based agriculture an animal is one part of the system, using inputs \(e.g. grass\), but creating inputs for other parts of the system \(e.g. manure\). This can be highly efficient.
1.2.1.4.1.1.8.9.2.3.1. Pro: In general these systems still result in net inefficiency making the point above still valid. At a very minimum, animals heat themselves, consuming energy which will never be reclaimed \(by manure or otherwise\), so meat will always be less efficient, though there are certainly steps that can be taken to minimize the inefficiency.
1.2.1.4.1.1.8.9.2.4. Con: We measure those conversation ratios [by burning the material](https://www.scientificamerican.com/article/how-do-food-manufacturers/) in question and measuring the heat outputs.  This is not a good analog for how our body processes those materials. The krebs cycle is a complex process that involves breaking and recreating chemical bonds with catalyst enzymes. Consuming all the amino acids needed to create a protein is not equivalent to consuming the protein. Protein folding is very complex and we don't fully understand how it works or the energy equations involved.
1.2.1.4.1.1.8.9.2.4.1. Con: We know that the caloric efficiency of the Krebs cycle is somewhere around 1/3, which is very, very close to the efficiency of burning. Not surprising as chemically they are similar. In order for this point to be relevant you would need to either see a caloric efficiency for meat consumption of 1 \(impossible\), or animals would need to somehow convert the feed they eat at an efficiency of 1 \(also impossible\). If both are somehow twice as efficient, the math still doesn't work in favor of meat.
1.2.1.4.1.1.8.9.2.4.2. Con: This may be true but we cannot rationally operate on what is not known. Ignorance is not a tool, we cannot use it to justify sweeping changes and cultural paradigm shifts. We can only operate on what we do know. Since we do not know, we cannot conclude anything one way or the other. It may indeed be better to consume meat.
1.2.1.4.1.1.8.9.2.4.2.1. Con: There is known harm in continuing to eat meat.
1.2.1.4.1.1.8.9.3. Pro: Permaculture based models of farming allow us to use land that wouldn't be arable for many other food products.
1.2.1.4.1.1.8.9.4. Con: There isn't enough pasture land available even if we were to clear the wilderness in all of North America. Factory farming and feeding animals grain and fishmeal etc doesn't exist in purpose to be harmful. Like all industries, it maximizes yield and minimizes inefficiencies. It is the most efficient, and yet compared to a plant based diet is extremely wasteful.
1.2.1.4.1.1.8.9.5. Con: It is true that the question of how we raise the animals is highly relevant. However the alternative “permaculture” would still reduce the meat production greatly \(while its quality would go up\) so we would still have to reduce our meat consumption.
1.2.1.4.1.1.8.9.5.1. Con: Do means justify ends? How is not quite so relevant when the end result is still the slaughter of animals. You cannot "humanely" kill en masse.  Indeed, giving a happy life to the herd only makes the inevitable that much more heartbreaking.
1.2.1.4.1.1.8.9.5.1.1. Con: This seems to be coming from a human-centric emotional perspective. From a utilitarian perspective - and arguably from the herd's perspective - it would indeed be better for them to have happier and more pleasant lives.
1.2.1.4.1.1.8.10. Pro: Animal agriculture contributes to an environmentally, socially and financially balanced agricultural system, with mixed feed and food crops, nutritionally diverse diets, reduced inputs and closed nutrient cycles, and a balance of operating risk from diversification.
1.2.1.4.1.1.8.11. Con: These same monocultural practices are also used to produce food for animals, thus creating a double impact. Cutting one half of this double impact would still reduce its impact.
1.2.1.4.1.1.8.12. Pro: The recycling of animal manures is an essential part of growing crops. The removal of this sorce of natural fertilizer would have a detrimental effect economically and environmentally on crop production and it would leave us totally dependent on artificial fertilisers.
1.2.1.4.1.1.8.12.1. Con: Animals can be farmed for reasons other than producing meat. This would allow for animal manure \(and other useful products\) to be harvested without involving the many other moral and practical issues of eating meat.
1.2.1.4.1.1.8.12.1.1. Con: To farm animals just for their manure would not be economically viable.
1.2.1.4.1.1.8.12.2. Pro: The production of farmed vegetables works much better when associated with animal farming because the feces of the animal are an essentiel aspect of having a healthy, fertile soil to grow food on on. We do need, however, to reduce our consomption or eat animal products rather than animals themselves to reduce our environmental impact.
1.2.1.4.1.1.9. Con: There is no moral imperative for the environment to remain in stasis or for us regard it as 'fundamentally' in need of preserving so. Heat death awaits the entire universe.
1.2.1.4.1.1.9.1. Pro: The "natural balance" was never stable, extinctions are as natural as the emergence of new species. Evolution is an ongoing process. It can be argued what the correct and moral action is for humans in any given situation, but species have been interfering with each other and forcing evolution since the beginnings of life.
1.2.1.4.1.1.9.2. Con: Environmental damage can cause harm and suffering to both humans and other forms of life - [up to and including mass extinctions](https://greens.org.au/magazine/national/what-even-global-anoxic-event). Even if the environment is not naturally static, there are other reasons to avoid environmental and ecological damage.
1.2.1.4.1.1.10. Con: In many cases land is best suited \(or only suited\) to farming meat; meaning that converting it to crops would be less efficient and thus cause greater environmental harms.
1.2.1.4.1.1.10.1. Pro: In harsh climates of the world, the transport and storage of fresh fruits and vegetables could cause ecological consequences that outweigh the benefits of ceasing local livestock production.
1.2.1.4.1.1.10.2. Con: More advanced farming methods allow previously unusable land to be utilized.
1.2.1.4.1.1.10.2.1. Pro: [Vertical farming](https://www.newyorker.com/magazine/2017/01/09/the-vertical-farm) could solve issues of scarce agricultural land.
1.2.1.4.1.1.10.2.2. Pro: Many methods of soil-free agriculture exist. Methods like aeroponics are used by [NASA](https://www.nasa.gov/vision/earth/technologies/aeroponic_plants.html) in space experimentally. The possible yields [rival](http://naes.agnt.unr.edu/PMS/Pubs/309_2017_03.pdf) the need for anywhere near as much farmland as is used today. The [microbes](https://ohioline.osu.edu/factsheet/SAG-16) in soil process the environment in ways that break them down into the same nutrients available through liquid nutrient solutions.
1.2.1.4.1.1.10.3. Pro: Not all land is good for planting human food, you need pastures. With pastures you can get herbivores to eat unedibe grass and transform it into eat into edible meat
1.2.1.4.1.1.10.3.1. Con: -> See 1.2.1.4.1.1.10.2.
1.2.1.4.1.1.10.4. Pro: Grasslands and plains make great areas to raise livestock but bad areas for farming due to lack of water or soil nutrients.
1.2.1.4.1.1.10.4.1. Pro: A lot of land for cattle is not suitable for crops, because \(for example\) it might be too rocky or uneven. To convert this land to productive plant use would be devastating to the landscape and contribute to issues such as topsoil erosion.
1.2.1.4.1.1.10.4.1.1. Pro: [Video evidence](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=aYmZYdznwMg) showing the condition of land much grass fed beef is herded on.
1.2.1.4.1.1.10.5. Pro: Farmers often rely on grazing animals to collect energy from a tropic level that cannot be accessed by humans. The meat gained from these animals has minimal costs to the environment yet is productive.
1.2.1.4.1.1.10.6. Pro: Village farmers often rely on their animals to store energy for their consumption over the winter or over dry seasons when harvests are poor.
1.2.1.4.1.1.10.7. Pro: Not all forms of terrain support intensive vegetable farming because the soil is poor, or the terrain too steep. Keeping animals in these areas displaces the need to raze new land for agricultural use.
1.2.1.4.1.1.10.8. Pro: Farming plants utterly destroys the environment. About 15% of the USA has been cleared, razed to dust, plowed, and replaced with planted crops. The damage to the environment is the total destruction of native plants and massive disruption of native fauna. Grazing is much less destructive. Concentrated feed lots use a negligible area by comparison. \(but depend on planted crops, the analysis of the problem is just not simple\).
1.2.1.4.1.1.10.9. Pro: We can raise animals in terrain that we cannot raise vegetables or grain. Areas such as steep slopes and high mountain sides can be successfully grazed in a sustainable, ecological way, but cannot be used for growing vegetables or grains without energy intensive and destructive modifications.
1.2.1.4.1.1.10.10. Pro: Responsible ruminant herd grazing programs actively combat desertification, which provides meat eaters a tertiary motivation to preserve green space.
1.2.1.4.1.1.10.11. Pro: Many natural rangelands are low-investment nutritional resources which can only be made useful to humans by using them to raise livestock.
1.2.1.4.1.1.10.11.1. Pro: Ruminants can eat grass which would be useless for the nutrition of humans otherwise.
1.2.1.4.1.1.10.12. Con: There is [far less land](https://www.onegreenplanet.org/animalsandnature/facts-on-animal-farming-and-the-environment/) where only and exclusively livestock can be raised than there is agricultural land where plant foods can grow. If we, therefore, use the land that was previously used for livestock to produce crops, we won't feel the need to even use those remote lands where only livestock can be fed.
1.2.1.4.1.1.10.13. Con: There are new [methods](http://www.bbc.com/future/sponsored/story/20170227-remote-control-farming) of farming \([hydroponics](https://bigpictureeducation.com/hydroponics-and-future-farming), etc\) that allow us to grow crops indoors or on the land where there was previously nothing at all, or just livestock.
1.2.1.4.1.1.10.14. Pro: Some level of animal husbandry is an integral part of a farm's nutrients cycle: from grass, to manure, to fertilizer. In alpine regions, where no crops grow, animals do not only increase the efficiency of the land but often are the most efficient way to use it to produce food. Thus, based on these two reasons, there is a justified level of animal husbandry \(which is far below current level\).
1.2.1.4.1.1.11. Pro: Plants can be grown in vertical farms, whereas animals cannot right now. Vertical farms use significantly less land through stacking.
1.2.1.4.1.1.12. Pro: Vegan foods offer little waste at the end since all of it could be turned to compost. This contrasts with the by-products of meat \(organs, fat, and bones\) that need to be re-purposed \(such as pet food\) for them not to get thrown away.
1.2.1.4.1.1.12.1. Pro: More and more animals are being killed every day. All the remains of these animals are dumped in fields or waste piles. All this killing is polluting the world.
1.2.1.4.1.1.13. Pro: Eating meat causes human suffering, particularly in poor communities. Health problems in communities living near to animal agriculture operations and psychological problems in abbatoir workers.
1.2.1.4.1.1.14. Pro: Current industrialized fishing is responsable for getting many species of fish and other sea animals near extinction if not extinct already, which is detrimental to ocean's ecosystem.
1.2.1.4.1.1.14.1. Pro: The population of sea birds has fallen almost 70% from 1950 to 2010 as industrial fishing has depleted the oceans' fisheries.[Seabirds suffering massive population declines](https://www.cbsnews.com/news/seabirds-suffering-massive-population-declines/)
1.2.1.4.1.1.15. Con: If the impact is local, then the decision to prohibit meat eating and/or production should be local. This argument is relevant only to local policy and has no bearing on the absolute morality of meat eating and production.
1.2.1.4.1.1.16. Pro: Avoiding eating animals will help global bio-diversity in a big way. No longer will the planet be saturated with a few breeds of cows, chickens etc.
1.2.1.4.1.1.17. Con: Regenerative livestock farmers worldwide are responsible for rehabilitating enormous tracts of land and in some cases helping bring at risk species of wildlife back into areas. I'm yet to see many grain and veg farmers to have a similar awareness.  [Stewardship with Vision](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=yW82TiCagbE&list=PLpv166NSeBHuOkeZRoGm2950uxIJCrHap) is just one of many such projects.
1.2.1.4.1.1.18. Pro: There's an overpopulation of farm animals which is likely to be ["the leading cause of modern species extinctions"](https://mercyforanimals.org/overpopulation-is-destroying-the-planet-but)
1.2.1.4.1.2. Pro: The common practices underlying the production of meat \(massive livestock farming\) are unethical.
1.2.1.4.1.2.1. Pro: Many animals raised for food are not slaughtered or kept humanely.
1.2.1.4.1.2.1.1. Con: Laws in states such as the UK require that animals be [stunned](https://www.rspca.org.uk/adviceandwelfare/farm/slaughter/factfile) before being killed, so as to be totally unconscious \(and thus incapable of feeling pain\) at the time of their slaughter.
1.2.1.4.1.2.1.1.1. Con: These same laws around pre-stunning do not apply to Shechita \(Jewish\) and Halal \(Muslim\) slaughter of animals for meat, whose [methods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ritual_slaughter) usually involve cutting the animal’s throat with a very sharp knife, often without pre-stunning.
1.2.1.4.1.2.1.1.1.1. Con: The 2013 report released by the UK's Food Standards Agency reveals that of the animals killed by Shechita or Halal methods, a relatively [small proportion](https://www.food.gov.uk/sites/default/files/2013-animal-welfare-survey.pdf) were slaughtered without stunning \(p. 4\).
1.2.1.4.1.2.1.1.1.2. Con: Shechita slaughter methods cause an instant drop in blood pressure in the brain that immediately results in [irreversible loss of consciousness](http://www.shechitauk.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/02/A_Guide_to_Shechita_2009__01.pdf) \(p. 3\). Thus, despite not pre-stunning, Shechita does not result in more animal suffering than other methods.
1.2.1.4.1.2.1.1.1.3. Pro: The Halal and Shechita presence on the Western meat market is [expanding](http://www.dialrel.eu/images/factsheet-market-uk.pdf); for example, Halal meat now comprises approximately 25% of the entire UK meat market, in comparison to 11% in 2001. Halal and Shechita methods therefore affect a significant \(and growing\) percentage of livestock destined for slaughter.
1.2.1.4.1.2.1.1.2. Con: The [Library of the European Parliament](http://www.europarl.europa.eu/RegData/bibliotheque/briefing/2012/120375/LDM_BRI\(2012\)120375_REV2_EN.pdf) asserts that there is no definitive scientific evidence that an animal \(or any sentient being\) does not feel pain whilst unconscious. Indeed, stunning may only stop an animal displaying pain. \(p.1\)
1.2.1.4.1.2.1.2. Con: The [Humane Slaughter Act](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Humane_Slaughter_Act) in the US has been a federal law for over 60 years. It decrees that the animal must be insensible to pain prior to slaughter.
1.2.1.4.1.2.1.2.1. Con: The [Humane Slaughter Act](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Humane_Slaughter_Act) does not apply to all animals. Chickens, for example, are not mentioned under the Act, despite the fact that over 9 billion chickens are slaughtered in the US annually.
1.2.1.4.1.2.1.2.2. Con: Legislation requires enforcement, which takes resources and may not always be consistent or effective.
1.2.1.4.1.2.1.3. Con: In Australia, there is a [legal standard](http://kb.rspca.org.au/how-are-animals-killed-for-food_477.html) that calls for "the minimisation of the risk of injury, pain and suffering and the least practical disturbance to the animals” during slaughter.
1.2.1.4.1.2.1.3.1. Con: -> See 1.2.1.4.1.2.1.2.2.
1.2.1.4.1.2.1.4. Pro: [Video evidence](https://www.thedodo.com/chickens-video-legal-slaughter-gordon-1043872702.html) has shown that chickens are slammed onto hooks for slaughter, scalded alive and suffocated during the slaughtering process at some factories.
1.2.1.4.1.2.1.5. Con: [EU legislation](https://ec.europa.eu/food/animals/welfare/practice/slaughter_en) calls for the use of properly approved stunning methods in order to minimise the pain and suffering of farm animals being slaughtered.
1.2.1.4.1.2.1.5.1. Con: -> See 1.2.1.4.1.2.1.1.2.
1.2.1.4.1.2.1.6. Pro: [Numerous](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/home-news/outcry-after-undercover-film-exposes-brutality-of-halal-industry-10019467.html) [undercover](http://www.huffingtonpost.com/stephen-wells/undercover-investigation-_b_8131212.html) [investigations](https://www.peta.org/features/butterball-peta-investigation/) have reported slaughter-houses' gross misconducts and abuses, which resulted in a total disregard for animals' welfare during the slaughter.
1.2.1.4.1.2.1.7. Pro: Nearly one million chickens and turkeys are [boiled alive](http://nation.time.com/2013/10/29/nearly-one-million-chickens-and-turkeys-unintentionally-boiled-alive-each-year-in-u-s/) each year in the U.S. alone.
1.2.1.4.1.2.1.8. Con: Humane slaughter is as fast and painless as possible. Death in the wild is almost always one of the following: Starvation, disease, freezing, scorching or being eaten alive.
1.2.1.4.1.2.1.8.1. Con: Not all slaughter methods used in the present day are humane.
1.2.1.4.1.2.2. Pro: Because egg and meat production are separated by modern production practices the males of egg-laying chickens are often [shredded](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chick_culling) shortly after hatching as they are not needed.
1.2.1.4.1.2.3. Pro: Raising animals in confinement, deprived of sunlight, natural soil and freedom to move, is cruel and against their nature.
1.2.1.4.1.2.3.1. Pro: In confined animal feeding operations \([CAFOs](http://vegetarian.procon.org/sourcefiles/pew_putting_meat_on_the_table.pdf)\) pigs have their tails cut off, chickens have their toenails and beaks clipped off, and cows have their horns removed and tails cut off with no painkillers.
1.2.1.4.1.2.3.2. Pro: [Baby cows](http://vegetarian.procon.org/sourcefiles/farm_sanctuary_welfare_of_cattle_in_beef_production.pdf) raised for veal are tied up and confined in tiny stalls their entire short lives \(3-18 weeks\).
1.2.1.4.1.2.3.3. Con: Industrial livestock farming might not replicate the natural environment of animals, yet it is an expression of the food chain in which humans dominate these animals. In this sense, it is quite natural.
1.2.1.4.1.2.3.3.1. Con: Food chains are not naturally given but socially constructed. Hence, some people see it as perfectly natural to not eat animals, for example some Buddhists for whom this would go against the [foundations of their world view](https://books.google.co.kr/books?id=s9BgCgAAQBAJ&printsec=frontcover&dq=Vegetarianism+and+Animal+Ethics+in+Contemporary+Buddhism+von+James+Stewart&hl=de&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwjZhYHU0-7MAhUBlKYKHRx6BigQ6AEIGzAA#v=onepage&q=Vegetarianism%20and%20Animal%20Ethics%20in%20Contemporary%20Buddhism%20von%20James%20Stewart&f=false).
1.2.1.4.1.2.3.3.2. Con: Food chains are merely descriptive, not deterministic. If we were to eat other humans today, our food chain would reflect that - but by no means prescribe or justify that we eat other humans. To argue that we have to eat meat due to our position in the food chain is thus a circular argument.
1.2.1.4.1.2.3.4. Pro: Animals are often given so little space that they cannot turn around or lie down comfortably.
1.2.1.4.1.2.3.5. Pro: Most factory-farmed animals have been genetically manipulated to grow larger or to produce more milk or eggs than they naturally would.
1.2.1.4.1.2.3.6. Pro: In contemporary intensive animal farming there is massive [use of antibiotics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antibiotic_use_in_livestock) and antimicrobials in livestock to treat infections as well as to promote growth and improve feed efficiency.
1.2.1.4.1.2.3.7. Con: Humans are part of nature, and hence what they choose to do and what to they actually do are not separable from nature. It is only perception that human is distinct from nature, but there is no fundamental basis for this.
1.2.1.4.1.2.3.8. Pro: The animals' nourishment in factory farming is often contrary to their natural one.
1.2.1.4.1.2.3.8.1. Pro: There are [cases](http://www.huffingtonpost.ca/2014/02/21/iron-maiden-hog-farm_n_4832104.html) in which pigs were fed with intestines of diseased piglets.
1.2.1.4.1.2.3.8.2. Pro: Herbivores like [cattle](http://www.mfablog.org/is-factory-farming-turning-cows-into-cannibals) are fed with feces, feathers, and feet of chickens.
1.2.1.4.1.2.4. Con: -> See 1.1.4.8.1.1.9.2.1.1.7.1.6.1.13.2.6.7.
1.2.1.4.1.2.5. Pro: Animals on farms are routinely [mutilated with no anesthetic.](http://freefromharm.org/animal-products-and-ethics/a-comprehensive-analysis-of-the-humane-farming-myth/) The animals are castrated, given nose rings and ear tags, dehorned and debeaked, all of which are painful exercises carried out on most farms.
1.2.1.4.1.2.5.1. Pro: De-beaking is a procedure in which one to two thirds of each hen's beak tip - which has similar [pain receptors](https://freefromharm.org/animal-cruelty-investigation/debeaking-video-shows-standard-practice-on-free-range-egg-farms/) to those found in human fingertips - is seared off without anaesthetic. It is reportedly standard practice on both factory and free-range egg-laying farms.
1.2.1.4.1.2.6. Pro: Cows have been seen to have incredibly strong bonds, particularly between mothers and their calves. On these farms the calves are often taken away from the mother to be killed and there are numerous reports of [mother cows calling and searching](https://www.peta.org/issues/animals-used-for-food/factory-farming/cows/hidden-lives-cows/) for their babies.
1.2.1.4.1.2.6.1. Con: This sentience is momentary and [ephemeral](https://news.nationalgeographic.com/2015/02/150225-dogs-memories-animals-chimpanzees-science-mind-psychology/), thus should not interfere in animals' ability to predict death and retain recent events. Parenthood problems could, therefore, be avoided by sedating and giving more "humane" treatment to animals.
1.2.1.4.1.2.6.1.1. Con: This claim essentially suggests drugging farm animals to make them forget their parents or children. This is not remotely humane.
1.2.1.4.1.2.6.1.2. Con: Even if animals cannot remember specific events, it's immediately apparent in any number of studies and situations that animals recognize each other, including parents.
1.2.1.4.1.2.6.1.2.1. Pro: There have been many experiments successfully performed that presuppose animals being able to form lasting bonds with each other, and many incidental observations showing ongoing bonds and relationships between specific animals.
1.2.1.4.1.2.6.1.2.2. Pro: Pet animals react differently to owners and other humans, and many will pine for missing or deceased owners - even when given everything they need in practice.
1.2.1.4.1.2.6.2. Con: This is a biological response rather than emotional. Mother cows udders become full of milk when the calf is removed, and the mother is looking for her calf simply for relief. The same is true of removed calves calling for their mothers for milk. Once the calves begin to eat solid food they stop searching for the mother, and once the mothers' milk production stops they stop searching for their calves. If there was an emotional response, we could assume some pairs would continue to search.
1.2.1.4.1.2.7. Con: -> See 1.1.4.8.1.1.9.2.1.1.7.1.6.1.13.2.6.6.
1.2.1.4.1.2.8. Pro: Cruel practices and incidents of animal abuse have been repeatedly exposed in animal agriculture. The true incidence is probably greater than those shown up through investigation.
1.2.1.4.1.2.8.1. Pro: My friend used to work in the poultry industry and personally witnessed extensive abuse of the animals on a daily basis.
1.2.1.4.1.2.9. Con: Stressed out livestock will produce lower quality meat and less of it. It is in farmers' best interests to make sure their livestock are comfortable and safe.
1.2.1.4.1.2.9.1. Con: Many current farming operations either do not realize or do not apply this principle.
1.2.1.4.1.2.10. Pro: Many meat producing companies use misleading titles like "Cage Free" and "Free Range" to make buyers feel better. Meanwhile instead of having individual cages that barely allow for movement, animals are crammed into huge pens where they often are so close that they bump into each other constantly. Either way you put it, it is inhumane and unnecessary to continue this practice for the sake of enjoying the taste.
1.2.1.4.1.2.11. Con: Unethical common practices suggest more ethical practices exist, and thus this is an argument to change practices not halt farming of animals.
1.2.1.4.1.2.12. Pro: Animals in the slaughter chute that sense mortal danger feel fear—terror, in fact. That fear manifests as rushes of adrenaline and other biological processes may lock that fear into the meat that carnivore humans eat. According Jess Zimmerman, writing in Atlas Obscura, botched slaughtering of pigs—the pig survives the first attempt to kill it—causes less saleable, bad-tasting, acidic “pale soft exudative” meat, i.e., pork from frightened animals. [Why Meat from Scared Animals Tastes Worse](https://www.atlasobscura.com/articles/why-scared-animals-taste-worse)
1.2.1.4.1.2.13. Con: This claim depends on examples of treatment that is cruel.  Many of the examples of farming practices viewed as cruel serve a function that benefits the animal's well being.  For instance calves are separated from their mother at birth because we are able to prevent infections better while they increase antibodies, killing is necessary but pain free, animals are restrained, but in many cases are free to exhibit normal social behavior. Producers know that reducing stress increases output.
1.2.1.4.1.2.13.1. Con: Their claim is supported by the existence of battery cages.
1.2.1.4.1.3. Pro: The meat industry is particularly harmful to its workers. Supporting the meat industry by eating meat involves being complicit in this harm.
1.2.1.4.1.3.1. Pro: Eating meat has negative impacts on [worker safety](http://www.mdpi.com/1660-4601/13/2/197/pdf). Meatpacking workers are injured at a rate of 27.6 per 100 workers - double the average of all manufacturing industries.
1.2.1.4.1.3.1.1. Pro: [Human Rights Watch](https://www.hrw.org/news/2005/01/24/abuses-against-workers-taint-us-meat-and-poultry) has called meatpacking "the most dangerous factory job in America".
1.2.1.4.1.3.1.2. Con: The meatpacking industry can be dangerous, but the solution for improving the safety of slaughterhouse workers is the strengthening of workplace safety rules, and increasing the monitoring of factories for safety violations.
1.2.1.4.1.3.1.3. Con: Safety in the US meat industry has increased drastically since the early [1990s](https://www.meatinstitute.org/index.php?ht=a/GetDocumentAction/i/46343).
1.2.1.4.1.3.1.3.1. Pro: That does not mean it is still not the most dangerous industry out there.
1.2.1.4.1.3.1.4. Con: The source cited refers to poultry but in other branches of the meat industry safety standards are very high and injuries in the US decreased from [20% to 6%](https://www.meatinstitute.org/index.php?ht=a/GetDocumentAction/i/83419/) in 2012.
1.2.1.4.1.3.1.5. Pro: Health problems for workers is [negative](http://file:///C:/Users/Gregor/Downloads/ijerph-13-00197%20\(2\).pdf) for the national economy at large and only profitable to the individual firm.
1.2.1.4.1.3.1.6. Pro: Meatpacking companies often [stop workers from getting compensation](https://www.hrw.org/news/2005/01/24/abuses-against-workers-taint-us-meat-and-poultry) for workplace injuries by denying or delaying complaints and not filing reports about injuries.
1.2.1.4.1.3.1.7. Pro: The low wages and benefits on meatpacking workers makes the injuries hard to treat.
1.2.1.4.1.3.2. Con: Workers in other food producing industries do not fare any better than workers in the meat industry.
1.2.1.4.1.3.2.1. Pro: Fruit and vegetable picking is an extremely [low paid](https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2016/aug/08/pick-fruit-veg-low-pay-brexit-vote-food-industry) and very hard job.
1.2.1.4.1.3.2.1.1. Con: Young Europeans on a gap year finance their trip by [fruit picking](http://workingholiday.co/fruit-picking-seasons-in-australia) in, for example, Australia. It therefore cannot be so hard as to discourage those travellers.
1.2.1.4.1.3.2.2. Con: Slaughterhouse workers have to kill living creatures on a daily basis, which has been proven to cause [severe psychological and physiological stress or PTSD](http://scholar.colorado.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=2157&context=honr_theses) \(pp. 20-25\), no matter whether the worker started the job knowing what it entailed.
1.2.1.4.1.3.3. Pro: The meatpacking industry is [made particularly hazardous](https://www.hrw.org/news/2005/01/24/abuses-against-workers-taint-us-meat-and-poultry) due to a lack of training being provided to workers, close working quarters and insufficient safeguards.
1.2.1.4.1.3.4. Con: This is a problem that should be solved through regulation of workplace conditions or temporary boycotts, not permanently ceasing to eat meat.
1.2.1.4.1.3.4.1. Con: There is nothing that can be done to prevent the need for carrying out certain psychologically and physically harmful tasks inherent to the meat industry: the killing of the animal by a human hand, for example, is inevitable, whatever workplace regulations are put in place.
1.2.1.4.1.3.4.1.1. Con: Animal slaughter can be mechanized.
1.2.1.4.1.3.4.1.1.1. Con: Both [Halal](https://special.worldofislam.info/index.php?page=Food/halal_haram/2_conditions) \(Islamic\) and [Shechita](https://www.myjewishlearning.com/article/kosher-slaughtering-an-introduction/) \(Jewish\) laws surrounding the proper slaughter of meat would be likely to [prohibit](http://halalfoodauthority.com/faqs) mechanised slaughter due to the rules surrounding who is fit to slaughter and how the slaughtering must occur.
1.2.1.4.1.3.4.1.1.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.1.4.1.2.1.1.1.3.
1.2.1.4.1.3.4.1.2. Con: The people who work in slaughterhouses likely do not care about killing animals, so employing them to do that is not mistreating them.
1.2.1.4.1.3.4.1.2.1. Con: [Studies](http://www.news.com.au/national/slaughterhouse-workers-are-more-likely-to-be-violent-study-shows/news-story/f16165f66f38eb04a289eb8bd7f7f273) have shown that working in animal slaughterhouses leads to desensitisation to suffering and violence in general: abattoir workers have aggression levels comparable to those of incarcerated people, and towns with abattoirs have higher rates of domestic violence and violent crimes including murder and rape. This suggests not only that the meat industry harms worker psychology, but that it indirectly harms the wider human population as well.
1.2.1.4.1.3.4.1.2.1.1. Con: It must also be considered that cause and effect could be reversed here. There could be many people with violent tendencies or desires who work in slaughterhouses in an attempt to give that violence a safer outlet.
1.2.1.4.1.3.4.1.2.1.1.1. Con: Regardless of this, it is still possible that working in slaughterhouses could exacerbate pre-existing violent tendencies.
1.2.1.4.1.3.4.1.2.1.1.1.1. Pro: One would have to avoid empathizing with the animals they must slaughter in order to avoid emotional pain when working in a slaughterhouse, which would likely involve avoiding or minimizing empathy entirely - which would in turn reduce one's empathy with other human beings and make one more likely to commit crimes or inflict violence against them.
1.2.1.4.1.3.4.1.2.1.1.1.2. Pro: Inflicting violence or wounds on animals is desensitizing to violence and wounds in general, as previously mentioned.
1.2.1.4.1.3.4.1.2.1.1.2. Con: Violent tendencies and desires should be managed and addressed, not indulged.
1.2.1.4.1.3.4.1.2.1.2. Con: Facilities regarded as undesirable to live near - such as abattoirs, power stations or prisons - are often built in locations that are already run-down and have a higher level of crime, thus these studies could say little about the true effect of slaughterhouses on local violent crime.
1.2.1.4.1.3.4.1.2.1.2.1. Con: Maybe facilities are built there because only those areas accept and support the practices of slaughterhouses. The situation is similar to the industries the US brought overseas because they could not accept the conditions they create \(such as leather tanning, clothing, or electronics\).
1.2.1.4.1.3.4.1.2.2. Con: -> See 1.2.1.4.1.3.2.2.
1.2.1.4.1.3.4.2. Pro: Even if temporary boycotts are necessary to spur this sort of change, that does not imply that humans should permanently stop eating meat.
1.2.1.4.1.3.5. Pro: -> See 1.2.1.4.1.3.4.1.2.1.
1.2.1.4.1.3.6. Con: This claim is a generalization. It is impossible to claim that the meat industry as a whole is harmful to its workers. Some companies may be harmful to its workers, but to apply this characterization to all companies in the meat industry is not logically sound.
1.2.1.4.1.3.6.1. Pro: This claim depends on an assumption that eating meat of any kind only supports large-scale meat production. There are many sources of meat that do not fit that description, and among those sources, harm to workers compares very well with peer operations in sustainable agriculture.
1.2.1.4.1.3.6.1.1. Con: Unless the demand for meat changes or several hundreds of thousands more small meat companies start a new business and remain stable, large-scale meat production will remain the primary means of meat production.
1.2.1.4.1.3.7. Pro: Slaughterhouse workers often suffer from serious psychological damage due to the violence involved in their job.[ptsdjournal.com](http://www.ptsdjournal.com/posts/the-psychological-damage-of-slaughterhouse-work/)
1.2.1.4.1.3.8. Con: This is an argument for another topic, namely, workers rights.
1.2.1.4.1.3.9. Pro: Working on a fishing boat is a very hard and dangerous job and ranks [second](http://www.forbes.com/sites/jacquelynsmith/2013/08/22/americas-10-deadliest-jobs-2/#3ea9405c5095) most deadliest in the US.

Pro: Poultry processing workers at big companies including Tyson, Pilgrim’s, Perdue and Sanderson Farms are denied [basic rights](https://upvoted.com/2016/05/27/we-wear-diapers-activists-behind-the-givethemabreak-campaign-reveal-the-horrific-conditions-of-poultry-workers/). They are not allowed to go to the toilet while working.
1.2.1.4.1.3.10.1. Pro: The workers are forced to wear [diapers](https://upvoted.com/2016/05/27/we-wear-diapers-activists-behind-the-givethemabreak-campaign-reveal-the-horrific-conditions-of-poultry-workers/) or to smudge themselves.
1.2.1.5. Con: Economists believe that [creative destruction](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/c/creativedestruction.asp) is pertinent for economic growth. 200 years ago [90% of the U.S. worforce](https://www.agclassroom.org/gan/timeline/farmers_land.htm) were farmers. If we hadn't let the farming jobs go, economic development would have been impossible and the rise of living standards would have been suppressed.
1.2.1.6. Con: Speaking of cliche's, the old buggy whip trope comes to mind. But as NYT and a guy named Mike Masnick, writing at TechDirt notes, many carriage-industry companies in 1890s found ways to adapt to the automobile market that could have put them out of business. Farmers can be helped to adapt.[Buggy Whips Not The Perfect Analogy Of Businesses Disrupted By Innovation?](https://www.techdirt.com/articles/20100110/1613597692.shtml)
1.2.2. Con: Intensive livestock farming is economically bad for small farmers.
1.2.2.1. Pro: As intensive farms are highly mechanized \(requiring little manual labor\) their introduction into rural areas tends to [reduce](http://thegreentimes.co.za/negative-impacts-of-factory-farming/) rural employment. In the U.S., for example, the number of farm workers has dropped 80% over the past 50 years.
1.2.2.2. Con: The negative effects of mass-production on smaller retailers is true of most industries, it is not particular to livestock farming.
1.2.2.3. Con: Intensive livestock farming is not the only option for small farmers. They can easily specialize in alternatives, such as organic livestock farming.
1.2.2.3.1. Con: It can be very costly, or economically impossible, for small farmers to change crops quickly without going bankrupt or into debt.
1.2.2.4. Con: Some farms are only useful for livestock rearing. For example farms on areas of clay which cannot be ploughed. The farmers can only earn a living on small farms if they rear livestock intensively.
1.2.3. Con: Whether or not something is good for the economy does not impact whether it is morally acceptable.
1.2.3.1. Con: Economic harms would leave many people worse off. This is a morally relevant consideration.
1.2.3.1.1. Con: Economies are not static, they can adapt and change. Where sales drop in meat products, they will likely rise in meat alternatives. Disruption could lead to more innovation and growth than traditional industries.
1.2.3.1.2. Con: Conflating the biological concept of harm \(meaning pain, distress, or the loss of quality of life\) with economics, is a major moral error that nullifies any argument with regards to "economic harm".
1.2.3.2. Pro: Slavery was also once essential for economic concerns.
1.2.3.2.1. Pro: "Economics" is simply the study of prices that arise from supply & demand. But morality in society determines what is allowed to be supplied or demanded. It is a major category error or a criminal fraud to pretend that because there is demand there must be supply, or vice versa. We don't think that way about offering children for sex abuse, for instance, or about nuclear fissile materials for bombs.
1.2.3.3. Con: Something that is not morally acceptable for one person may be morally acceptable for another. Suggesting that eating meat is morally unacceptable completely negates human history and the fact that, at one point, we needed to sustain off of it. However, this is not to say that there aren't more humane ways to treat cattle / livestock.
1.2.3.3.1. Con: Human history includes the development of morality. Immoral things, or things now considered to be immoral, were once widely accepted in the past \(e.g. slavery\). This does not "negate history". Therefore, it is plausible that eating meat will come to be considered immoral \(by more people, and perhaps society in general\) in the future.
1.2.3.3.1.1. Pro: Slavery was once accepted by the society and their culture. This does not mean it is morally justified.
1.2.3.3.1.1.1. Pro: Slavery, much like eating meat, involves harm to living beings which demonstrate clear preferences towards avoiding it.
1.2.3.3.2. Con: Depending on the moral system used, morality can depend on the situation. Even if eating meat was morally acceptable at some points in human history, it does not necessarily follow that it is morally acceptable to continue eating it today. We are less dependant on meat than we once were, and more able to reasonably replace it.
1.2.3.3.2.1. Con: The second statement is a non sequitur from the first.
1.2.3.4. Pro: Harvesting human organs and reselling them would be good for the economy. This does not make it morally acceptable, and this debate centres around the morals of the action, not its economic viability.
1.2.3.4.1. Con: Whether introducing an industry which is immoral might be good for the economy does not detract from the contention that stopping an established industry would be bad for the economy.
1.2.3.4.2. Con: The economic impacts of stopping the consumption of meat has a moral implication under utilitarian ethics in so far as negative economic conditions have the potential to cause harm.
1.2.3.5. Pro: Slavery once was a big part of the Global Economy but was abolished even though the economic issues caused by it, there was bigger problems then just the economic part and it is the same here, there are bigger problems now, such as forest devastations, climatic effects, and the fact that we are using animals as slaves
1.2.4. Con: Keynesian economic theory deals pretty well with large fluctuations in commodities.

1.2.5. Pro: Ceasing to produce meat would be extremely harmful to low-income countries.
1.2.5.1. Pro: The production of farm animals provides an [economic safety net](https://www.voanews.com/a/decapua-farm-animals-29mar12-144898655/179917.html) for many people in poor countries.
1.2.5.1.1. Pro: Keeping livestock helps [reduce seasonal variability](http://www.fao.org/docrep/v8180t/v8180t07.htm) in food supply due to seasonal variations in crop productivity.
1.2.5.1.2. Pro: Animals act as both a secure form of wealth, and as an emergency supply of food in case of shocks to the normal food supply \(like droughts or famines\).
1.2.5.1.3. Con: The economic benefits are far outweighed by the risk to [long-term health](https://nutritionfacts.org/topics/milk/) and cost of potential medical expenses if the use of animals is relied on.
1.2.5.1.3.1. Con: Based on the assumption that the affected people economically depend on their farm animals, a discontinuation would drastically worsen their situation and ultimately, potentially lead to undernutrition, which has a very strong [negative impact on health](https://www.ncoa.org/healthy-aging/chronic-disease/nutrition-chronic-conditions/why-malnutrition-matters/10-ways-malnutrition-impact-your-health-6-steps-prevention/), as well as on [child development](http://www.orphannutrition.org/understanding-malnutrition/impact-of-malnutrition-on-health-and-development/).
1.2.5.2. Pro: Farmers in low-income countries are typically poor, and would not have the resources to transition to a different occupation or type of farming if they were no longer able to buy livestock.
1.2.5.2.1. Con: Any significant changes in worldwide meat consumption would likely, in practice, be gradual, and allow farmers plenty of time to adjust.
1.2.5.3. Con: The economies of low-income countries are [particularly vulnerable](http://blogs.lse.ac.uk/usappblog/2016/09/10/climate-change-impacts-the-economic-development-of-low-income-countries/) to climate change. Insofar as stopping meat production helps alleviate climate change, it is beneficial for the economies of low-income countries.
1.2.5.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.4.8.1.1.9.2.1.1.7.1.6.1.
1.2.5.4. Con: Low-income countries suffer from malnutrition and food shortages. Since ceasing to eat meat would allow us to produce more food, this would benefit low-income countries.
1.2.5.4.1. Con: Geographical types are different. Some places, such as mountains, are much better to keep livestock in than to grow crops.
1.2.5.4.1.1. Pro: Farming animals is often the best — or only — choice available to farmers in certain areas; meaning less natural land needs to be converted to agriculture.

1.2.5.4.2.1.1. Pro: [About 60%](http://ajcn.nutrition.org/content/78/3/660S.full)  of US pasture land suffers from overgrazing, which rapidly erodes the soil and makes it unfit for future agricultural production.
1.2.5.4.2.1.2. Pro: About [70%](http://www.worldwatch.org/peak-meat-production-strains-land-and-water-resources-1) of the world's agricultural land is used for animal pastures.

1.2.5.4.2.1.4. Con: The organic manure produced by animals is useful in the growing of crops.

1.2.5.4.2.2. Pro: Given current methods of agricultural production, the world will be unable to meet growing food demand. It is therefore urgent that we increase agricultural productivity by producing less meat.
1.2.5.4.2.2.1. Pro: Agricultural land [worldwide](http://www.scidev.net/global/food-security/feature/sustainable-food-production-facts-and-figures.html) suffers from soil erosion and loss of soil nutrients, which make the land unfit for future agricultural production.
1.2.5.4.2.2.2. Pro: By 2050, the world will have to produce [50% more food](https://www.theguardian.com/sustainable-business/food-blog/10-things-need-to-know-global-food-system) than it does now.

1.2.5.4.2.2.5. Con: There is a reasonable chance of large scientific innovations, like the [Green Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Green_Revolution), drastically boosting our agricultural productivity without necessitating the removal of meat from our diets.

1.2.5.4.2.2.6.2. Con: From an ecological point of view, increased food availability for a given species, globally, results universally in an increase in species population.  Population control can only verifiably and assuredly be effected using those same ecological principles of controlling or capping a food supply to sustain x population at equilibrium or possibly a gentle, gradual rate of decrease.
1.2.5.4.2.2.7. Pro: The amount of crops grown for the meat industry is not efficient. Meaning we could feed the entire population of the US with less than half the crops used to feed the animals which are then slaughtered. 1.2.5.4.2.2.9. Pro: [Meat is less efficient for acquiring vitamins than it used to be](http://baltimorepostexaminer.com/carnivores-need-vitamin-b12-supplements/2013/10/30). This causes people to overeat foods to make up for the inadequate dietary intake, which amplifies the growing food demand.
1.2.5.4.2.3. Con: There are various ways to exploit existing agricultural resources more efficiently to meet food demands.
