1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.10. Con: Not all vegetarians have a healthy diet. There is a lot of meatless junk food available. Hence, an absence of meat in a diet does not equal a healthier diet.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.10.1. Pro: Common vegetarian food can easily contain more saturated fat than meat products:[many cheeses](https://www.google.co.uk/search?q=saturated+fat+in+cheese&oq=saturated+fat+in+cheese&gs_l=psy-ab.3..35i39k1j0i7i30k1l5j0l2j0i20i263k1j0.109157.109157.0.109378.1.1.0.0.0.0.74.74.1.1.0....0...1.1.64.psy-ab..0.1.73....0.I8SP0mw2-eM) contain double \(or more\) the amount of saturated fat that steak contains per 100g.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.10.2. Con: Obviously there is plenty of room for variation in the health outcomes of both vegetarian and omnivorous diets. However, on average, vegetarian diets are more healthy.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.10.3. Pro: A good reason for why meats are unhealthy is the artificial means meat is raised, the hormones, etc.  Similarly, pesticides on vegetables are unhealthy too. Comparing vegetables to meats obfuscates the real causes of health detriments.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.10.4. Con: That is not an argument for eating meat, but for eating more healthy food, which is clearly possible, and will become more easy as the range of vegetarian food available increases
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.10.5. Pro: A heathy vegan diet is theoretically possible, but it is extremely hard to maintain such a diet long term without consuming any animal products, unless you take an industrial preconceived super balanced food.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.10.5.1. Con: With an increase in vegetarians and vegans, there would be more allowances made and techniques developed to assist in maintaining these diets, making it easier and healthier to maintain them.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.10.5.2. Pro: Certain professions \(i.e. wildland firefighting\) require high body mass and muscular strength which can be logistically difficult, biologically disruptive, and expensive for some individuals to maintain on a vegetarian/vegan diet.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.10.5.3. Pro: Sports and high-performance diets count on high intakes of meat to build muscle and make elite performances possible. Vegetarian supplements such as soy [can have some negative health effects](https://www.nutrition.org.uk/healthyliving/an-active-lifestyle/eating-for-sport-and-exercise.html?limit=1&start=2).
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.10.5.4. Pro: An omnivorous diet consisting of mostly vegetables supplemented by a small to moderate amount of meat is not much different to a full vegetarian or vegan diet, yet is more balanced and convenient because you are not going out of your way to avoid something completely.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.11. Con: The [fact](http://qz.com/91123/vegetarians-live-longer-but-its-not-because-they-dont-eat-meat/) that vegetarians live longer \(up to nine years\) is not only due to their meatless diet, but also to the fact that they are more likely to exercise, smoke less and drink less alcohol.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.11.1. Pro: Correlation does not indicate causation.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.11.2. Pro: This misconception is the result of a self-selection bias. Basically, vegetarians are all people who are unusually interested in their health to begin with. Thus, naive studies will tend to show that vegetarians are healthier regardless of whether vegetarianism is healthy.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.11.2.1. Con: There is no guarantee that all vegetarians are necessarily people who are unusually interested in their health to begin with. People may choose a vegetarian lifestyle out of moral concerns, or many other reasons.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.12. Con: When looking at smaller sample sizes and more diverse diets there is little to suggest that meat is inherently unhealthy.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.12.1. Pro: The Inuit people in Russia, US, Canada, and Greenland [survive primarily off of meat while suffering fewer health problems](http://discovermagazine.com/2004/oct/inuit-paradox) than modern nations; even with their high animal fat and protein diet.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.12.1.1. Pro: The Innuit people consume a diet suited to the natural adaptations made over generations to their digestive tracts. Diets of the Western and developed world can potentially be less healthy as they introduce access to a variety of foodstuffs to which individuals' digestive tracts are unlikely to be accustomed.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.12.1.2. Con: Comparing the lifestyle and diet of the Inuit people to that of most meat consumers is a false equivalency. The source of their meat is vastly different from industrially produced meat found in grocery stores, and their daily lives are vastly different from the average meat consumer. Even when adjusted by age, race, smoking, exercise, personal income, educational level, marital status, alcohol, and region, [studies](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4073139/) have shown that plant based diets lead to reduced health risks.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.12.2. Con: The lifestyles of these groups are radically different from normal human populations in very many ways. There is no reason to conclude that their meat consumption in particular is what gives them a health advantage.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.12.2.1. Con: There is no baseline for 'normal human populations' . Every established ethnic and regional group has adapted their diet to their environment and available resources. Changes to these diets result in negative health effects and so whilst eating meat does not confer any advantage, ceasing its consumption will inflict a negative effect on their health.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.12.2.2. Pro: For the group tests to be valid, they should also be checked for malnutrition in general, and also be tested with vegan equivalent supplements that offer a similar dose of iron, protein and other nutrients that those groups' existing diets are lacking.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.12.3. Pro: The Masasai tribe in the Kalahari desert [are healthy yet primarily eat meat and Mongongo nuts](http://sciencenordic.com/maasai-keep-healthy-despite-high-fat-diet).
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.12.4. Con: By definition, the sample sizes are smaller, and therefore less predictive of dynamics governing the overall population.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.12.4.1. Con: There is no need to predict the characteristics of the whole population. As long as identifiable groups can be shown to benefit from meat consumption, that is sufficient to prove that not everyone should stop eating meat.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.12.5. Pro: The science of diet is far from settled. This is a complex topic that will take many decades, if not centuries to understand. The advent of quantum super computer arrays will undoubtedly speed our understanding of diet, but today, there is much to learn about nutrition and heath. To suggest otherwise is to be dishonest about the topic. Take the science of salt consumption as a recent example. Today, it is now known that the dangers of too little salt vastly out weigh the dangers of too much.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.12.6. Pro: A study of 544 seven-year-old children in Kenya who previously had a vegan diet found that: 
"The group that received the meat supplement showed the biggest improvement in fluid intelligence over the two years, and those who had either milk or energy supplements were better than the controls. The group that received the meat supplements were more active in the playground, more talkative and playful, and showed more leadership skills," \([Link](https://www.theguardian.com/society/2005/feb/22/food.sciencenews)\)

1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.12.8. Pro: Humans are accustomed to eating meals containing meat. The meals not only gives us proteins that are important for several bodily functions but also even the people who are eating vegetables don't necessarily become healthier. You become healthier based on you intake of foods that your body needs, which may include meat. [Studies](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4073139/) have shown the reduced risk of diseases from plant based diets, even when adjusted by age \(i.e., attained age as time variable\), race, smoking, exercise, personal income, educational level, marital status, alcohol, and region.
1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.13. Pro: Meat consists of a lot of protein. Therefore, its consumption leads to increased intake of protein. [The China Study](https://www.amazon.co.uk/China-Study-Comprehensive-Nutrition-Implications/dp/1932100660) measured the ideal daily intake to be 8 to 10 percent, whereas most people consume twice as much.

In poor societies practicing vegetarianism, it often correlates with malnutrition. 1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.14.1. Pro: [Even nowadays the meat is still needed for human survival](http://hungerreport.org/2017/wp-content/uploads/2017/08/hunger-report-2017-full.pdf). 1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.19. 1.3.1.7.4.3.3.3.20. Pro: Though the optimal diet may include some meat, in the real world non-vegetarians eat [way too much of it](https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2018-01-02/have-a-meaty-new-year-americans-will-eat-record-amount-in-2018). Don't let the perfect be the enemy of the good.

1.3.6. Pro: Moving from the consumption of meat in totality would signal a move from prioritizing our biological predispositions, as highly adapted persistence hunters, towards an emphasis on valuation of all life without regard for the occupied stratum of consciousness of the consumed. This is contradictory because of the difficulty in the determination of self-awareness of many types and complexities of life-forms, plants included, thus creating a difficulty in the determination of an objective boundary.
1.3.6.1. Con: This argument commits the [fallacy of equivocation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equivocation). By not explicitly defining "self-awareness", the ambiguity surrounding the term allows for equivocation on the ideas of self-awareness and consciousness. The argument may thus suggest there is no "objective boundary" between plant and animal life in this regard, while "Self-awareness", properly defined, would preclude such an argument based on our knowledge of the neural substrates of [introspection/self-referential thought](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1364661313002118).
1.3.6.2. Pro: From a natural perspective: In the global ecosystem \(and any smaller one\) death is of equal importance to life. Animals rarely die of old age because they are still within the food chain. If they did the ecosystem would be severely unstable. Predators must kill to survive and keep populations in check that might otherwise reduce important plant numbers. They're a necessary part of the balance.
1.3.6.3. Con: You assume that we are "highly adapted hunters" which is not true. Being technologically advanced so we can hunt is not the same as saying we are good hunters. Having a very good capacity of reasoning and abstractization makes you a good hunter as well as it makes you a good anything else. Using our intellect as humans we could easily bypass our so called "need" for meat. Also, being hard to determine is not the same thing as being contradictory.
1.3.6.4. Con: There is no "objective boundary" between good and evil. All boundaries are subjective and are yet no less compulsory as such. To assume that we need an objective boundary to make ethical decisions problematizes ethics into circuity.
1.3.6.5. Pro: As a species we are good hunters, not only as a result of our superb reasoning skills, but because of possession of many biological  adaptations, which are specific to carnivorous animals. Humans are for example able [to outrun every animal in long enough distance](http://www.slate.com/articles/sports/sports_nut/2012/06/long_distance_running_and_evolution_why_humans_can_outrun_horses_but_can_t_jump_higher_than_cats_.html).
1.3.6.5.1. Con: There are other plausible explanations for how that specific adaptation could have been acquired or emerged - it could equally be used to aid escape from predators, or cover more ground while searching for food, or - as the article also alludes to - beating other scavengers to a meal.

1.3.6.7. Pro: From an 'unnatural' or human perspective: The farming of animals is not contingent on our natural dispositions. Being done outside the natural ecosystem it does not overfeed predators nor bloat prey populations in a way that affects natural food webs.

1.3.8. Con: Just because something comes naturally to humans, it does not mean it is the correct decision to do it.
1.3.8.1. Pro: If this were a valid argument, it could equally be made in favour of genocide, rape, torture, destruction of ecosystems, slavery, suppression, etc. \(see other great apes, our closest relatives, for inspiration\).
1.3.8.1.1. Con: There is no claim that genocide, rape, torture, slavery a.s.o. are natural to humans \(who live in a "natural" way\). This is a fallacy.
1.3.8.2. Pro: The fact that eating meat is natural or inherent to human culture does not imply that we ought to eat it. According to Hume, it is a fallacy to make claims about what [ought to be](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Is%E2%80%93ought_problem) \("we ought to eat meat"\) on the basis of statements about what is \("it is natural to eat meat"\).
1.3.8.2.1. Con: 'What is' is a better indicator than philosophical thoughts on 'what ought to be' because it has shown its applicability.
1.3.8.2.1.1. Con: Every progressive change throughout history involved people collectively taking action to change "what is" in favor of their view of "what ought to be".

So if we look at "what is", we discover that it *is* the case that there exists an extensive proven track record of successful cases where "what ought to be" has led to positive cultural change that we value today.

So by this point's own reasoning, we should therefore take "what ought to be" seriously.
1.3.8.2.2. Con: Humans ought to do what it is in their nature to do.
1.3.8.2.2.1. Con: What is "human nature" is widely debated.
1.3.8.2.2.2. Con: This could be argued to include any result of conscious, deliberated choices - such as not eating meat. Humans are also social animals, so social consensus decisions could also be argued to be part of "human nature".
1.3.8.2.2.3. Con: No. A bully beating up a weaker kid to feel powerful is acting according to human nature too. Gorging on high-calorie food is acting according to human nature. Human nature is sometimes good, sometimes evil. Morality must be sensitive to human nature, true. But the entire reason we need morality as a subject of study is that *sound* moral reasoning doesn't come naturally to us.
1.3.8.2.3. Pro: Additionally, we should seek to get the relationship between what is *normative* and what is *normal* the right way around. The normative should dictate what is normal. Just because an act is normal does not in and of itself signify that the act is therefore good, just, beautiful, or useful.
1.3.8.2.3.1. Con: Suggesting that a normative action should drive a definition of normality implies that the action can be based solely on a simple rational, moral or lifestyle choice. The amount of meat consumed is unnatural and is therefore at odds with the foundation of this cultural argument.

1.3.19.6. Pro: Human dentition is designed to mechanically process both meat and vegetables. Millions of years of evolution have shaped us with canine teeth to be able to pierce meat. We have genetic code for enzymes that process meat in our DNA and have been omnivorous since the earliest human relatives. The problem is not the humans should stop eating meat, the problem is the process for how the meat makes it to our plates.
1.3.19.6.1. Pro: Biologically speaking, the human dentition provides information on the proportions of different types of food we should be ingesting, and 1/8 of it should be meat \(4 canines out of 32 teeth\).
1.3.19.6.1.1. Con: Exact numbers of teeth are not relevant, as evolution does not "plan ahead" in this manner or deliberately create specific ratios, rather creates or further emphasizes traits which prove useful. Humans would develop exactly as many canines \(and molars\) as they needed at the time, so the exact numbers or ratios do not prove what is described here, as it is possible that having more molars is more beneficial in ways that having more canines is not. \(Also, molars are still used to chew meat.\)
1.3.19.7. Pro: Eating meat has created social structure from group hunting which helped with intelligence and creating societies. We are who we are because we eat meat.

1.3.19.9. Pro: The human body was designed to hunt and eat meat.
1.3.19.9.1. Pro: The Macronutrient Protein breaks down into 9 essential amino acids \(required from food because we cannot create these amino acids\). Most meats \(chicken, beef, fish, pork\) have ample quantities of these. Most vegan/vegetarian diets lack these essential amino acids without consuming large quantities of soy, peanuts, or beans - foods associated with inflammation, digestive issues, and allergy.
1.3.19.9.2. Pro: Humans are omnivores and our digestive system is built to tackle most types of food. In most aspects of life, humans are jacks of all trades, masters of none. We don't excel in terms of eyesight, smell, speed, reaction, strength or anything else but our bodily functions are just good enough to deal with most challenges. The only thing we excel at is brainpower. This versatility and one "superpower" has made us masters of this world. Including the ability to digest most foods.
1.3.19.9.3. Pro: Humans are designed to be omnivorous, based on dental evolution, bipedalism for hunting, and eyes designed for depth discrimination to track prey. Stopping the consumption of what our bodies were designed to process could have ill effects on over health of the human animal.
1.3.19.9.4. Con: -> See 1.1.4.8.1.1.9.2.1.1.7.5.1.
1.3.19.9.5. Pro: Eating meat is literally in our DNA. We evolved as omnivores and our bodies are dependent on outside sources for proteins as a result. Our digestive systems, and dentition, even the placement of our eyes and ears on our skulls all suggest a body designed for an omnivorous diet.
1.3.19.9.6. Pro: Animals which evolved to hunt have their eyes facing forward, for depth perception to capture prey, whilst herbivores have their eyes on the sides of their faces for wider field of view but sacrificing depth perception, that they don't need since they don't hunt.
1.3.19.9.7. Con: To say that something in nature is "designed for" anything is giving a teological nature to the object. Things in nature have a purpose, not a goal, thus the human body is not designed for eating meat, rather it has evolved in a way that suggests it can.
1.3.19.9.8. Pro: Humans are mammals, and evolved to be carnivorous.  Our physiology demonstrates this. That does not imply that humans can't survive on vegan diets; they can, with supplements. Some societies still value that trait, others don't. What is wrong, is imposing your views on others who have a different take on the subject.[Forbes Article on the Subject](http://bit.ly/2lPA6kg)
1.3.19.10. Con: Whilst we may have been hunter-gatherers in the past it does not mean we should continue to be. Continuing to be hunter-gatherers on the basis that we were known as hunter-gatherers is an [Appeal to Tradition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Appeal_to_tradition)
1.3.19.10.1. Con: A specific mode of nutrition is no tradition; otherwise, all living beings have traditions. Rather, it is a naturally \(evolutionary\) developed way of behavior. Therefore, this is no appeal to tradition but an appeal to our nature.
1.4. Con: Farming vegetarian products also directly or indirectly harms animals in the process of crop production and harvest.
1.4.1. Pro: Millions of animals are [killed](http://abcnews.go.com/Technology/story?id=97836&page=1) every year to allow for appropriate land to grow crops such as wheat and barley.
1.4.1.1. Con: The unintentional deaths of animals such as mice during the production of harvesting crops is very different to intentionally breeding animals such as cows for slaughter.
1.4.1.1.1. Con: If we are considering animals as living beings worthy of rights, they are still relevant.
1.4.2. Con: A [study](http://freefromharm.org/common-justifications-for-eating-animals/comparing-animal-deaths-production-plant-animal-foods/) done on how many animals die to produce 1,000 calories of a number of different food sources found that for vegetables it is only 2.55 animals, while for beef it is 29 animals and for chicken it is 251.1. This suggests significantly fewer animals are harmed in the production of vegetarian products.
1.4.2.1. Con: That study relies on a 'best case' farming practice that seeks to minimise animal deaths and protect crops through all stages of vegetable production. In almost all cases those practices are not followed in mainstream agriculture.
1.4.2.2. Con: That study included, "animals slaughtered directly for food, and animals killed in the harvesting of crops... predators killed in defense of livestock, animals prevented from existing by the destruction of their habitat, and human deaths from food poisoning, disease, or accidents on farms or in slaughterhouses."

It does not include intentionally killed pest animals, which would likely make a difference for both sources, especially if they included insects, which they did not.
1.4.2.3. Con: While the study suggests fewer animals are harmed in production of vegetarian products, the authors themselves recognize limitations in their own study. They clearly state that no accurate estimates exist of how many animals are actually harmed due to farming practices.
1.4.2.3.1. Con: There is no bounded study without limitations. It is better to have some indication than none.
1.4.2.4. Con: The study talks about calories. Vegetables are more "calorie dense" \(as in calories/kg\) than meats. If we look at the numbers for protein production, the result would be the opposite, since meat is much more protein dense than vegetables. Calories alone are not sufficient for a healthy diet, and protein malnutrition can be fatal.
1.4.2.4.1. Con: "Meat is much more protein dense than vegetables" is true only when comparing the average of all meats with the average of all vegetables. Some vegetable products like hemp seed and tempeh provide comparable amounts of complete proteins in comparable serving portions, while also providing other minerals and nutrients not typically found in meat. A vegan diet \(+B vitamin\) does not preclude adequate nutrition, nor does an omnivorous diet protect against malnutrition. [healthyeating.sfgate.com](http://healthyeating.sfgate.com/tempeh-vs-meat-4276.html)
1.4.2.4.2. Con: The calorie is a more basic and important indicator of nutrition than protein content.
1.4.2.5. Con: The study also does not take into account broader environmental impacts of plant agriculture like destruction of animal habitat for farmland, leaching of agricultural chemicals \(even fertilizer leaked into nearby areas like water source can cause negative impacts like increased algae growth which can choke out fish species\), and removal of insects which are often basal components of food webs of that area.
1.4.2.5.1. Con: All of these are important issues, but these issues are made worse in the raising of animals for food.  This point ignores the amount of plant nutrition necessary in order to sustain livestock for human consumption; that amount is vastly more than if humans obtained their needed calories from plants directly. A large percent of calories and protein are lost in the conversion of plant-based animal feed into meat during the life of the animal that is being harvested.[Feed-to-food conversions](http://iopscience.iop.org/article/10.1088/1748-9326/11/10/105002)
1.4.2.6. Con: Animals per calorie is an absurd metric. 251.1 zooplankton are insignificant compared to 2.55 blue whales.
1.4.2.7. Con: This is a severely misleading characterization of the study. A single cow provides far more than 1000 calories. A single pound of hamburger contains 1000 calories. [www.myfitnesspal.com](http://www.myfitnesspal.com/food/calories/generic-ground-beef-patty-1-4-lb-85-15-45681048)
1.4.3. Pro: Converting land to make it suitable for harvesting soybeans and other vegetable products results in [habitat loss](http://wwf.panda.org/what_we_do/footprint/agriculture/impacts/habitat_loss/) that threatens entire ecosystems.
1.4.3.1. Pro: [Oil palm plantations](http://wwf.panda.org/what_we_do/footprint/agriculture/impacts/habitat_loss/) in Indonesia and Malaysia pose significant threats to endangered elephants and tigers.
1.4.3.1.1. Con: Vegans and vegetarians do not consume a significant amount more palm oil than meat-eaters
1.4.3.1.2. Con: Plant oil is consumed by so many uses that it is independent to a choice between eating meat or the alternatives.
1.4.3.1.3. Con: Conscientious avoidance of unsustainable palm oil sources is another issue.
1.4.3.1.4. Con: These animal habitats are in danger due to lacking environmental protection and also from illegal activities.
1.4.3.2. Con: Animal agriculture also requires large amounts of land, and thus also causes habitat losses.
1.4.3.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.5.4.2.1.2.
1.4.3.2.2. Pro: -> See 1.2.5.4.2.1.3.
1.4.3.2.3. Con: There are many ways to raise animals for slaughter on the same land that is also used to grow plant-based foods, and some sustainable models suggest that cattle and other grazing creatures would be an essential part of a sustainable food system. Nicolette Hahn Niman suggests ungulates are crucial regulators of soil health, for example.

[www.latimes.com](http://www.latimes.com/books/jacketcopy/la-ca-jc-nicolette-hahn-niman-20141123-story.html)
1.4.3.2.3.1. Con: Growing the food for the animals that are raised for slaughter requires a large land area that cannot be used for food for humans. [The land area](https://online.science.psu.edu/bisc002_sandbox_4938/node/5024) needed to grow plants to feed animals to feed humans is much larger than the land area needed to grow plants to feed humans.
1.4.3.2.3.2. Con: Ungulates do play a role in regulating soil health, but farmers can do a much better job at this while producing more food.
1.4.3.2.3.2.1. Pro: Ungulates do prevent the erosion of topsoil but the number of ungulates per unit area is not enough to provide a sustainable meat source.
1.4.3.3. Con: The vast majority of the global production of soybeans, alfalfa, corn and about 40 percent of all grains is used as feedstock for animals in CAFOs.
1.4.3.4. Con: -> See 1.2.5.4.2.3.7.1.
1.4.3.5. Con: Land conversion occurs due to industrial activity and is not guided by an argument for meat or alternatives.
1.4.4. Pro: In many cases, even [farming basic grains kills 25 times more sentient animals](https://theconversation.com/ordering-the-vegetarian-meal-theres-more-animal-blood-on-your-hands-4659) \(as measured against per kg of usable protein in the final product\) than the equivalent farming practices for red meat.
1.4.4.1. Pro: Ploughing and harvesting kill small mammals, snakes, lizards and other animals in vast numbers.
1.4.4.1.1. Con: Small animals have far less sophisticated consciousness than most farm animals, so it is less morally wrong to kill them.
1.4.4.1.1.1. Con: This logic leads then to the proposal that we eat more chicken and less beef, or that we eat more mice instead of chicken or beef. But there is no way to calculate the level of consciousness of a chicken compared to a cow and then multiply that by the number of chickens needed to replace the meat in one cow.
1.4.4.1.1.2. Con: This is not certain. Elephants are far larger than humans, but also widely acknowledged to be less intelligent, even if more intelligent than "average" animals. Without first-hand insight into the thoughts and consciousness of any given animal, all we have is guesswork from observed behaviour.
1.4.4.1.1.2.1. Pro: [Rats display a broad variety of interesting and sophisticated behaviour.](http://www.ratbehavior.org/RatBehaviorMain.htm)
1.4.4.2. Pro: [Millions](https://theconversation.com/ordering-the-vegetarian-meal-theres-more-animal-blood-on-your-hands-4659) of mice are poisoned in grain storage facilities every year and millions more are killed during 'mice plagues' that often affect crop areas.
1.4.4.2.1. Con: -> See 1.4.4.1.1.
1.4.4.2.2. Con: Meat production requires large amounts of grains to feed the animals that comprise it, this means that a large portion of these small animal deaths is a side effect of cultivation that only exists due to the breeding of animals for meat production
1.4.4.2.3. Con: Poisoning and killing mice may not be the only way to prevent them from interfering with grain production - natural barriers and repellents may be implementable, for instance.
1.4.4.2.4. Con: Poisoning pest animals is on its own an ethically questionable farming method \(due to both the deaths of animals and potential health effects on humans\), and one which ethical and organic farms may choose not use.
1.4.4.3. Pro: Red meat animals feed on pasture, which requires next to no upkeep. In contrast, farming vegetables requires the use of pesticides that kill massive amounts of insects and/or similar measures that kill birds.
1.4.4.3.1. Con: 99% of red meat animals in the United States spend at least some portion of their lives in a feedlot.  The grains in the feedlots have at least the same likelihood of requiring the use of pesticides, killing insects/birds, etc.
1.4.4.3.2. Con: Most red meat production is not free grazing.
1.4.4.3.3. Con: Dismissing the most destructive example of vegetable farming by citing the least destructive animal feeding practice does not dismiss the best vegetable farming practices \(hydroponics, intensive indoor growing, etc\).
1.4.4.3.4. Con: Farming animals that provide red meat, such as cows contributes to large amounts of deforestation.
1.4.4.4. Con: Measuring against kg of usable protein is misleading, since meat has higher concentrations of proteins relative to other nutrients. So the gap between meat and grains is not that large.
1.4.4.4.1. Con: I think the opposite is true - because meat has higher protein density, relatively more e.g. grains used for human consumption is needed \(in this context for covering dietary protein needs\), and so the alleged loss of animal life is relatively larger, if one accepts the premise \(that livestock consume more on pasture than harvested crops in feedlots\)
1.4.4.5. Con: 99% of animals raised for red meat spend at least some of their lives on feedlots. The grains they are fed are just as responsible for killing sentient animals.
1.4.4.5.1. Con: The article refers to Australia where that is not the case due to the vast amount of grazing space for cattle.
1.4.4.5.2. Con: The percentage is exagerated, and the origin of the animal protein will determine that, since in some productions such as cattle in most South America or Australia \(example of the article\) the same pasture stays in the soil for over ten years, something that only happens in vegetable production if it is utilized a permaculture system.
1.4.4.6. Con: That article only refers to Australia where cattle spend significantly more time grazing in fields. That does not hold for animals in factory farms fed a diet from farmed plant products.
1.4.4.7. Con: The article linked to is full of misinformation. Check the comments section for refutation of nearly every point. One point is the many mice being killed by harvest of grains, yet  "the only fields a mouse might be attracted to would be wheat or corn. Since both of those are harvested by machines which cut the stalks several inches above the ground. Any self respecting mouse would have no problem avoiding the harvester unless it is suicidal."
1.4.4.7.1. Con: The article does not talk about deaths of mice from harvesters, it talks of deaths of mice from pesticides to protect the crop.
1.4.4.8. Con: Modern urban farming using techniques such as [hydroponics](http://www.hortidaily.com/article/1177/900-crops-of-lettuce-per-square-meter,-and-only-4-laborers-per-hectare), aquaponics, and microfarming does not directly or indirectly harm animals, and yields far greater crop yields than traditional farming techniques.
1.4.4.8.1. Con: These more modern techniques [frequently require much more energy than their traditional alternatives](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4483736/). This indirectly harms animals and humans.
1.4.4.8.2. Pro: These novel farming methods provide more land for wildlife to roam, live, survive, and thrive by using less land to grow food on.
1.4.4.8.3. Pro: These methods tend to be in vertical gardens, which greatly reduce the conflicts between wildlife and farmers \(which often lead to predators, such as wolves, being shot\).
1.4.4.9. Con: This claim and the supporting article are very specific to beef cattle reared in Australia where pastoral lands are abundant. It doesn't hold when you consider the land required for free range animal rearing to satisfy the global appetite for meat.
1.4.4.10. Con: This depends on the food. Hand-picked crops like strawberries and peas do not harm as many animals — if anything they support life because beneficial insects are brought to the farm to help the crops grow. [Some foods like rice are used as habitats for birds](https://www.scidev.net/global/farming/multimedia/the-rich-diversity-of-birds-in-rice-field-ecosystems.html).
1.4.4.10.1. Con: The world's massive food-intake requirements cannot be met by a small subset of hand-picked crops.
1.4.4.10.1.1. Con: Is there evidence that this is true? We could ramp up production if people switch over from meat.
1.4.4.10.1.2. Pro: Hand-picked crops are more expensive and time-consuming
1.4.4.10.1.2.1. Pro: These factors might lead more crops to be produced in foreign countries where labor is cheaper but so are the standards. The product might have more chemicals in them that are not safe.
1.4.4.10.1.3. Con: Hand-picked crops could be supplemented with other crops to be viable to a world population
1.4.4.10.1.4. Con: The world already has difficulties providing food-intake requirements with non-hand-picked crops. Eating only hand-picked crops will be the solution instead of a barrier.
1.4.4.10.1.4.1. Pro: Food travels many miles \("food miles"\) to reach a destination. This raises food prices where some people cannot afford it. If everyone immediately eats food they picked \(such as if they grow their own\), then the food miles go to 0, thus making it much cheaper.
1.4.4.10.1.5. Pro: Hand-picked crops are [dangerous for workers](https://www.npr.org/sections/thesalt/2015/09/07/437285894/4-labor-intensive-crops-farmers-wish-they-had-robots-to-harvest), making it not viable.
1.4.4.10.1.6. Con: Automation could possibly improve over time where it becomes as humane as hand-picking. Then more options will open up.
1.4.4.10.1.7. Pro: With the laws that encourage food waste right now \(such as [USDA grades](https://books.google.com/books?id=JkrdDAAAQBAJ&pg=PT127&lpg=PT127&dq=usda+grades+food+waste&source=bl&ots=bx8H6maJdM&sig=0QKpJLFWvXMKC3DgKTRwlZWu-B0&hl=en&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwiN34rlpa7ZAhVG8WMKHSRaA6QQ6AEIaDAH#v=onepage&q=usda%20grades%20food%20waste&f=false)\), hand-picked crops do not seem a viable option.
1.4.4.10.1.7.1. Con: If laws and people change their habits where they eat food that does not meet standards \(such as "[ugly produce](http://www.endfoodwaste.org/ugly-fruit---veg.html)"\), then less food is wasted and hand-picking will be a more viable option.
1.4.4.10.2. Pro: Hydroponic and aeroponic crops that are hand-picked on the spot are both more humane \(indoor farming tends to have less animal issues\) and less resource-intensive \(more land for wildlife instead of agriculture and less driving = less roadkill\)
1.4.5. Con: Since animals hurt by vegetarian farming are hurt as an unintended consequence, while producing meat necessarily involves killing animals, the former is moral and the latter is not.
1.4.5.1. Pro: According to the [Doctrine of Double Effect](https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/double-effect/), if a harm is just an unintended consequence of a good action, then that good action can be morally permissible.
1.4.5.2. Con: At the point those consequences are systemic to mainstream agricultural practices they can't really be held to be unintended; they are known and consistent consequences.
1.4.5.3. Con: It doesn't matter if the consequence is intended or not, it still causes harm.
1.4.5.4. Pro: There's an ethical difference between intentional killing and indirect killing.
1.4.5.5. Con: Many of the animal deaths associated with vegetative farming are specifically intended, in order to protect crops and improve both yield and quality of harvest.
1.4.5.5.1. Pro: Pesticides are frequently \(and indiscriminately\) sprayed over crops, many of which also harm humans directly or indirectly.
1.4.5.6. Pro: The line of thought of deontologist philosophers supports this morality.
1.4.5.7. Con: The teleologist philosophers line of thought supports that the later is immoral since is produces what is not intended.
1.4.5.8. Con: In as much as the production of other crops does not intend to kill animals, the intent of producing meat is also not to kill animals. The fact that the animal has to die in order for us to eat it is just as much a side effect as the mouse that has to die for the grain to be harvested and stored.
1.4.6. Pro: -> See 1.3.1.7.2.
1.4.7. Pro: Killing pests - not just insects but large, intelligent animals - is a necessity on farms of any kind.
1.4.7.1. Con: Insects do not have the same capabilities as farm animals to feel pain and suffer. [Do insects feel pain? - A biological view](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007%2FBF01963580?LI=true)
1.4.7.1.1. Con: Suffering is very different from pain. Farm animals don't generally suffer, and even creatures that don't experience pain can suffer.
1.4.7.2. Pro: Deer, Elk, Antelope, rabbits, and wild pigs are just a few examples of larger mammals that wreak havoc on crops, and which are regularly killed to protect crops.
1.4.7.2.1. Con: Not on all the farms which is what the previous claim suggests. With many new forms of farming \(example: [en.wikipedia.org](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertical_farming)\) none of the animals which are larger than insects have to be hurt in the process.
1.4.7.2.2. Con: True enough, but it doesn't seem to be in nearly the same magnitudes.
1.4.7.3. Con: Pests can be repelled or physically barred rather than killed in many situations.
1.4.7.4. Pro: There is no food source that does not involve the so-called cruel and immoral treatment of animals. All vegetable crops have natural predators, and these predators are harmed and killed in the process of producing these crops for human consumtion.
1.4.7.4.1. Con: True enough, but it doesn't seem to be in nearly the same magnitudes.
1.4.8. Con: Land must be cleared both for farming crops and rearing livestock, so the cumulative damage done by vegetarian farming is still lower.
1.4.9. Pro: When we eat vegetables we are taking away food that animals could possibly eat. This could harm them in the long run.
1.4.9.1. Con: Through innovative new farming technology, such as hydroponic [vertical farming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertical_farming), food for animals can be produced in a very effective and sustainable manner.
1.4.9.2. Con: Nature itself has a way of regulating wild life population. In the long run the population of for example cows will decrease, ensuring enough food.
1.4.9.3. Con: Our produce is overwhelmingly grown in farms, not harvested, and thus not taken out of nature and away from animals. If you meant that any fruit that's eaten by a human was stolen from animals because it could have theoretically been gifted to an animal, than you'd have to consider eating anything stealing from every other animal on Earth, including humans.
1.4.9.4. Con: Generally feeding animals farm-grown produce is thought to have harmful affects on both the animal and human populations of a region when we could allow nature to self-regulate its populations. Animal feeding could potentially be used to lessen the immediate impact of widespread deforestation on some species, but generally this would be drastically less efficient than choosing not to destroy their habitats in the first place and would only slow the local animal population's imminent demise.
1.4.10. Con: Over time indirect animal deaths from farming could be reduced, if this was an explicit goal.
1.4.10.1. Pro: Certain plant crops are easy to grow hydroponically such as lettuce. Hydroponic growing method harm less insects that randomly fly onto the plants because they are grown indoors. This method is better than raising meat because meat ends lives no matter the method.
1.4.10.1.1. Con: Even indoor hydroponic farms suffer from major pest infestations, which require control measures. 
[Indoor Garden Pests](http://www.just4growers.com/stream/pests-and-diseases/the-5-most-hated-indoor-garden-pests.aspx) 
[Hydroponic Pathogens](https://www.maximumyield.com/parasitic-pathogens-infectious-diseases-in-hydroponics/2/1317)
1.4.11. Con: The argument is an example of [Whataboutism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Whataboutism) aka [Tu quoque logical fallacy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tu_quoque), and therefore does not directly refute the argument that eating meat is wrong.
1.4.11.1. Pro: Saying that a practice also causes a negative is not a counter argument. Everything effectively can be shown to create negatives. The issue is not if the practice is pristine and only creates positive outcomes, but the degree to which it creates less negatives. Animal agriculture creates more harm for animals. Animal agriculture includes all the harm of plant based agriculture plus the direct harm of farm animals. Farm animals are fed the plants, and much more than it would take to feed humans.
1.4.11.2. Con: In this case, this argument would refute that eating meat is wrong if it were true, since eating plants and fungi is the only alternative to eating meat.
1.4.12. Con: Food production for humans will always directly or indirectly disturb the ecosystem of other animals. If we take that as a given then we have to ask ourselves what types of harm to animals and the broader ecosystem are acceptable tradeoffs when producing food.
1.4.12.1. Pro: Ethics is not about absolutely eliminating harm, as that is currently impossible, but minimizing it and maximizing benefit.
1.4.13. Con: Raising farm animals ultimately kills more plants than raising plant foods, due to the amount of feed required to raise the animals.
1.4.13.1. Con: But you are operating under the assumption that you are correct that suffering and death is an unacceptable end, and therefore humans must not participate. If you are wrong, and we are arguing that you are, then the only thing that really matters is whether or not we raise plants and animals in an ethical environment where they will likely suffer less and die more peacefully than their wild cousins. If you are right, then all life is immoral and we must all die now to avoid further suffering.
1.4.13.1.1. Con: Actually, the arguments suggest that we should minimize suffering as much as reasonably possible, not take unreasonable steps \(such as exterminations\) to eliminate it. Ceasing to eat meat may or may not be part of optimally reducing harm and suffering.
1.4.13.1.1.1. Con: [This claim has been addressed.](https://www.kialo.com/eating-meat-is-wrong-1229/1229.0=1229.1+1229.21-1229.1404-1229.3032-1229.3298/-1229.3298)
1.4.13.1.1.1.1. Con: And that claim is itself debated.
1.4.13.2. Pro: Cows, as an example, will graze approximately [2% to 2.3%](https://beef.unl.edu/cattleproduction/forageconsumed-day) of their own weight in grass each day.
1.4.14. Con: Insects are not what many people would consider "sentient animals", although they technically are. "Minimizing" the harm to a smaller volume of animals, or to animals from a specific phylum is sort of splitting hairs.
1.5. Con: As animals lack rights it is morally permissible to raise them for slaughter.
1.5.1. Pro: Animals are unable to conceptualise of morality therefore they do not deserve to exist within our moral community.
1.5.1.1. Pro: Animals do not offer rights to one another. Animals hurt and eat one another.
1.5.1.1.1. Con: Animals do in fact offer rights to one another. You see this everywhere from [ants](https://www.terminix.com/blog/education/what-is-an-ant-colony) to [chimps](http://www.chimpworlds.com/chimpanzee-social-structure/). Animals maintain complex social hierarchies both within \(linked sources\) and across \(all symbiotic relationships\) species.
1.5.1.1.2. Con: The display of "rights" can be clearly portrayed if one were to observe a pack animals that cooperate such as wolves.
1.5.1.1.2.1. Con: Most if not all forms of "social" animal groupings are dominated by an [alpha animal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alpha_\(ethology\)). Therefore animals only know one right: the right of the strongest.
1.5.1.2. Pro: All and only human beings have the [capacity to reason](http://www.animal-rights-library.com/texts-m/regan01.htm). This is a definite feature of moral judgement. As animals lack this ability they are not morally equivalent.
1.5.1.2.1. Con: -> See 1.1.4.8.1.1.1.1.1.1.3.1.
1.5.1.2.2. Con: By this logic babies, little children as well as severely mentally disabled people cannot be possessors of rights either. But that would be wrong.
1.5.1.2.2.1. Con: Infants and disabled humans are participants in the human moral community, and so they possess rights.
1.5.1.2.2.1.1. Pro: Infants and disabled people are usually valued by family members or caretakers.
1.5.1.2.2.1.2. Pro: Infants and severely mentally disabled people are part of our species, and deserve respect on that basis.
1.5.1.2.2.2. Con: Animals do not have the potential of ever being part of the human community, whereas the mentally disabled and little children do.
1.5.1.2.2.2.1. Con: Domesticated animals and pets exist.
1.5.1.2.2.2.1.1. Pro: People consider their pets as part of the family.  Therefore they are part of the community.
1.5.1.2.2.2.1.2. Pro: Animals work and contribute to the community in various roles from guide dogs, sniffer dogs, horses to pull and transport etc.
1.5.1.2.2.2.2. Con: There are many cases of disability where there is no possibility of becoming morally independent and part of our 'moral community'
1.5.1.2.2.2.3. Con: This is a tautology as animals cannot be part of the human community because the human community only consists of humans.
1.5.1.2.2.2.4. Pro: It is selective morality to assign some species as a food source, and others as pets.
1.5.1.2.2.3. Pro: If conceptualisation of morality was a precondition to 'exist within our moral community' and to have the right to live and not be tortured and slaughtered, it would become permissible to harm pets, members of different moral communities \(different cultures\), people in a coma, with dementia and many others
1.5.1.2.2.4. Pro: Sentience is not moral justification for enslaving other beings across species-lines, any more than it is moral justification for enslaving ones own species who biologically lack cognitive abilities due to medical disorder. What about the taxonomy of a being permits otherwise morally egregious behavior?
1.5.1.2.2.5. Con: Babies, little children, and the mentally disabled do have substantially fewer rights than healthy adults. At the extreme end, a fetus can be aborted and people with extreme brain damage can be taken off life-support without their consent. Similarly, the animals should be provided a limited set of rights \(which they are, though I'll admit it should be expanded\) but I don't see anything substantially worse about a cow being slaughtered at a young age than dying of a disease at old age.
1.5.1.2.3. Con: When you confund "reason" with "human way of reasoning", you loose too much of a richness of the phenomenology: Every and each organism has its own Umwelt. Reason is not "human reason"--that works with calculus. There are a lot of "reasons" around.
1.5.1.2.4. Con: "Reason" cannot be a parameter for moral judgement, because it gives us nothing about the moral status of a subject. Using "reason" as criteria for moral status is like using the capacity of breathing underwater or flying with  your own wings for the same ends.
1.5.1.3. Pro: Animals are unable to enter into a social contract, and thus cannot be possessors of rights.
1.5.1.3.1. Pro: Humans in a society gain rights by the virtue of duties they have. Animals are unable to fulfill duties and therefore cannot enjoy the rights attached to them.
1.5.1.3.1.1. Con: Animals are responsible for important social tasks as well, e.g. guide dogs or [therapy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animal-assisted_therapy) animals. Humans depend on animals with this functions.
1.5.1.3.1.2. Con: Human rights are not granted to humans based on any duties but, as the name implies, simply because they are human.
1.5.1.3.2. Con: Animals possess certain rights.
1.5.1.3.2.1. Pro: In 2008, a committee of Spain's national legislature became the first to vote for a resolution [to extend limited rights](http://www.nytimes.com/2008/07/13/weekinreview/13mcneil.html?_r=2&ref=science&oref=slogin) to nonhuman primates.
1.5.1.3.2.2. Pro: In 1999, [Public Law 106-152](http://www.pet-abuse.com/pages/animal_cruelty/crush_videos.php#ixzz2C6yKSljR%3E) \(Title 18, Section 48\) was put into action in the US. This law makes it a felony to create, sell, or possess videos showing animal cruelty with the intention of profiting financially from them.
1.5.1.3.2.3. Pro: In 2015, the New Zealand Parliament unanimously passed the [Animal Welfare Amendment Act](http://www.loc.gov/law/foreign-news/article/new-zealand-animal-welfare-legislation-recognizes-animals-as-sentient-bans-cosmetic-testing/) \(No. 2\) 2015, which includes an amendment to the long title of the principal legislation, the Animal Welfare Act 1999, in order to specifically recognize animals as sentient.
1.5.1.3.3. Con: The social contract is a theoretic construct anyway, since no person literally entered it by signing anything.
1.5.1.3.3.1. Pro: Since contracts require consent to be legitimate, and no one has meaningfully consented to the social contract, the social contract has no force as a theory of morality.
1.5.1.3.3.2. Con: Even if the social construct is purely theoretic, it is a good way of deliberating about moral demands and duties.
1.5.1.3.4. Con: -> See 1.5.1.2.2.
1.5.1.3.5. Pro: Moral duties do not exist intrinsically. They are constructed and bestowed upon us through our participation in society.
1.5.1.3.6. Con: Animal Rights Theory claims, in its most part, negative rights for animals, i.e.: the right not to suffer, not to die, not to \(...\). Some non-paradigmatic humans may have no positive rights, but they still have their negative rights guaranteed.
1.5.1.3.6.1. Con: Negative rights for humans arise from an agreement not to violate the negative rights of others. Animals are not capable of entering into such an agreement.
1.5.1.3.6.2. Con: Animals are not capable of long run projection, they do not suffer in anticipation to death.
1.5.1.3.6.2.1. Con: They are, however, capable of suffering from being restrained, poor living conditions, over-crowding, injury and illness, mutilation without anaesthesia, etc.
1.5.1.4. Con: -> See 1.5.1.2.2.
1.5.1.5. Con: Nonhuman animals are unable to engage in sophisticated moral reasoning. Therefore, nonhuman animals cannot be held to sophisticated moral standards.

Humans on the other hand can engage in sophisticated moral reasoning. Therefore, humans can be held to sophisticated moral standards. This includes standards about how we should or should not behave towards nonhuman animals.
1.5.1.6. Con: Animals can conceptualize morals. Dogs and monkeys, for example, have a concept of fairness. Experiments have been done where two monkeys \(or dogs\) are given different treats for the same trick and those receiving the lesser treat for the same work will become agitated, angry or refuse the treat, even though one bad treat should be better than none.
1.5.1.7. Pro: Animals act on instinct and cannot deliberate between actions, therefore they have no moral status. For example, a tiger cannot be made to stand at court for killing a man, neither can a dog. If they can have no regard for human, we cannot have any regard for them. Therefore we can kill animals for food.
1.5.1.8. Pro: Humans don't have a moral responsibility to those who they don't think would exhibit the same moral responsibility were they in the same position. You have moral responsibility to a child because they will one day be an adult and you can reasonably expect them to treat you morally. I do not expect cows to have morals besides not being pointlessly sadistic. They would eat me if they could and were hungry, so I feel no qualms about eating them.
1.5.1.8.1. Con: But if your point was true, then we should not have a moral responsibilty to a disabled person that will never be able to be "just as moral as you".
1.5.1.9. Con: You are not required to be a moral subject in order to become a moral object. Utilitarism, for instance, is not based on the ability to conceptualise right or wrong but by the ability to feel pain or pleasure.
1.5.2. Pro: The fact that humans have developed to a greater extent than animals means that they should have the ability to use animals for their own ends.
1.5.2.1. Con: Most humans are more developed than infants or severely disabled people. This does not imply that it is ethically permissible to use infants and severely disabled people for any purpose.
1.5.2.1.1. Con: Barring a few exceptions, humans in most cultures have drawn a sharp line between what is permissible with fellow humans and with other species. We have created laws and customs, "suspended" the law of nature in order to ensure peaceful coexistence with each other. It is by no means obvious that this should, or even could, be extended to other species.
1.5.2.1.1.1. Con: Plenty of natural species do benefit from cooperation and peaceful coexistence outside of their own species. Symbiosis is a widely acknowledged facet of nature. Furthermore, there is no reason to believe humans could not use their reason to further benefit from "unnatural" and "artificial" symbiosis, nor is there any reason to suggest humans should not altruistically use such "artificial" symbiosis to improve the lives of other animals, as long as any downsides are appropriately managed.
1.5.2.2. Con: Suggesting that humans should have the ability to use animals for their own ends suggests that they haven't developed to a greater extent than other animals.
1.5.2.3. Con: We wouldn't like to be enslaved or killed by a more advanced alien civilization or a much more biologically complex alien species. So we shouldn't do this to animals.
1.5.2.3.1. Pro: It has not been disproven that we are not cattle by spiritual beings or aliens too advanced for us to understand. It would be presumptuous to think we are at the top of evolution in the universe.
1.5.2.4. Pro: While animals do have some rights, there is no equality between humans and animals, and our behavior to them depends on various criteria such as "is it harmful" or "is it useful". Some animals we try to eradicate, some we keep as pets, some we use for work or we use their wool / milk / meat.
Nobody cares if we kill rodents that carry diseases and affect crops; it's hypocritical to only "protect" the animals that we enjoy eating.
1.5.2.4.1. Con: To morally distinguish between humans and animals, a distinguishing factor must be found, or else it's simply an [anthropocentric bias.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anthropocentrism)
1.5.2.5. Con: Having a higher state of development does not permit humans the right to do any harm whatsoever.
1.5.3. Con: Humans should not have the authority to make decisions about animals given we do not have perfect information about their experiences.
1.5.3.1. Pro: Although it is harder to empathize with something you cannot intuitively understand at a glance via biological instincts, this does not mean the other party is not a living being which should have rights - merely that most humans are inherently biased in a specific way.
1.5.3.2. Con: There exist times when beings must make decisions that profoundly impact other beings, even thought the experiences of the affected parties cannot be known on a conscious level.
1.5.4. Pro: Unlike wild animals, farmed animals are born explicitly due to human action. While we may have a duty to ensure that their lives are not unnecessarily cruel, it is not immoral to kill them for our own ends given they would not have existed otherwise.
1.5.4.1. Pro: As long as these animals are given a reasonable quality of life, it is preferable that they get the opportunity to exist given to them by humans for the purpose of farming. In the alternative, many animals do not get to exist at all.
1.5.4.1.1. Con: People who don't eat meat give animals a better quality of life while avoiding the majority of conditions and situations that are caused by meat producing farmers. Thus, the terms of existence are better managed by the people that consider sentience above digestion. Most noticeably, the inferior existence and situation is massively created by industrially breeding domesticated animals into being.
1.5.4.1.2. Con: Bringing an entity into existence does not mean that you have the ability to use that entity for whatever purpose you want.
1.5.4.1.2.1. Pro: All humans are also born due to the actions and decisions of their parents. It is not subsequently moral to treat them however you wish to meet your own ends. Once a living being is alive, they should be subject to the same standard of morality that empathy drives us to apply to human children. Empathy naturally decreases the further from our species an animal gets, but we have empathy for them regardless.
1.5.4.1.2.2. Pro: That's like saying it's a parent's right to be abusive by virtue of causing their child's birth. The basic rights of any living creature are not affected by the circumstances of its birth.
1.5.4.1.2.3. Pro: This renders the concept of birth as giving claim to another life as being property to do with as one pleases. Ownership/property mentality as a form of colonialism was used for generations to rationalize the abuse of animals, children, women, other races, religions, under the concept that another is property to the person, group or country who feeds, employs or "owns" it, putting that life under their control. We have since implemented laws to counter abuses perpetrated under this philosophy.
1.5.4.2. Con: The lives of most animals in factory farms are plausibly worse than not existing, so it is immoral to bring them into existence.
1.5.4.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.1.4.1.2.3.
1.5.4.2.2. Pro: Certain diseases become more prevalent due to the cramped conditions in "factory" farming.
1.5.4.2.3. Pro: Chickens are commonly de-beaked without anaesthesia, a process which is extremely painful for them.
1.5.4.3. Con: It is immoral to bring creatures into existence in order to use and kill them. For example, it would be immoral to give birth to new children and use them as slaves or food.
1.5.4.3.1. Con: This is because humans have special, higher-order rights - for example, a right to freedom. Animals do not have these rights, so it is permissible to breed them for certain uses.
1.5.4.3.1.1. Con: Human rights only emerged through generations of human individuals failing to seek or achieve those rights. Rights are achieved by ending oppression. As exemplified by historical freedom stories, the worthiness of animals for rights is the matter under examination when weighing the morality of killing them for food.
1.5.4.3.1.1.1. Con: The mere fact that human rights were created through the removal of oppression does not mean that animals are analogous to previously oppressed groups of humans.
1.5.4.3.2. Con: This is an anthropomorphic analogy.  It is a trait of human psychology and one in this case which is incorrect.  Comparing a human child to a feed lot animal is unbalanced in value. A feed lot animal if left in it's natural condition consumes plants and in turn is consumed by other animals. If humans were not eating them they would be eaten by other predators/scavengers in the wild. Their condition in life does not change much between feed lot and field.
1.5.4.3.2.1. Con: Boredom \(such as that caused by monotonous, unchanging, restricted enclosures\) can also be suffering, and can cause stress or frustration in animals \(which can in turn lead to them attacking each other, especially when enclosures are also too small\). At the very least, it reduces the quality of their lives.
1.5.4.3.2.2. Con: As discussed earlier, these animals are bred specifically for human consumption. If humans had not created these animals, they could not possibly "be eaten by other predators/scavengers in the wild."
1.5.4.3.2.3. Con: "Unbalanced in value" is dubious. By what objective measure of value are you using? You are basing the value of a life on their potential to achieve some human-centric measure of worth. A feral human left in the wild, or a mentally handicapped child, is of equal value to a penned sheep then.
1.5.4.3.2.4. Con: Who is to argue when anthropomorphising is or isn't wrong? Blacks were enslaved because they were just brutes. Then one day we anthropomorphised them. Voila, suddenly rights are bestowed.
1.5.4.3.2.4.1. Con: Many indigenous peoples had broad arts, culture, and tradition, and sophisticated societies involving living in harmony with the land, other animals, and each other. Often this was a reasoned process developed and passed down through generations \(much like our own technology!\), not merely instinctual. Deliberately moderating your group's consumption in the name of sustainability, for instance, is hardly a "brutish" trait or culture. It is not "anthropomorphizing" to recognize genuine virtues.
1.5.4.3.2.5. Con: -> See 1.3.1.7.4.1.1.1.1.2.1.1.
1.5.4.3.2.6. Con: For this statement to be true every animal which is prey would end their lives at the hands of a predator. In fact most survive to experience a natural cause of death.
1.5.4.3.2.6.1. Con: Every animal is consumed by a scavenger of some kind though, some even microbial in nature, as originally stated. Therefore there is little difference between humans eating animals, and other wild life. If it is not immoral for a predator to eat prey, then it is not immoral for humans to eat animals as well.
1.5.4.3.2.7. Con: Anthropomorphism is the attribution of human characteristics to animals. There is no such attribution in the claim.
1.5.4.3.2.7.1. Con: Domesticated animals are at risk of predators/scavengers.  Predator management and protection is a core concern of raising livestock.
1.5.4.3.3. Pro: Granting sentient existence just to revoke it is arguably the cruelest act one can rationalize. That is "playing god" to its worst possible meaning.
1.5.4.3.4. Pro: -> See 1.5.4.1.2.
1.5.4.4. Pro: If we cease eating meat then millions of animals will never be born at all. We cannot be certain that animals would prefer to never be born than to die prematurely.
1.5.4.4.1. Con: An animal that isn't born can't prefer to be born at all as they don't exist. An animal that exists currently doesn't want to die prematurely \(they actively avoid pain\). Since we know they don't want to die \(avoid pain\) it is ethical not to harm them. If we create animals to kill \(harm\) we are acting unethically.
1.5.4.4.2. Con: It is true that animal intelligence exists and should be considered no matter where it scores relative to ours. Since we do not know if they prefer to live a little before being killed, we should not assume they would, especially because factory farming living conditions are not even good in the first place.
1.5.4.5. Con: The farming process is far from cruelty-free. As unanimous ethical farming seems to be impossible in our global capitalist climate the easiest way to ensure ethical treatment of animals is to take part in a widespread movement to stop supporting farmed meat.
1.5.4.6. Con: -> See 1.5.4.1.2.
1.5.4.7. Pro: Domestication was a "social contract" between the two species: humans obtained a mobile source of food and cloth and the animals were assured basic necessities and protection from natural predators and a guaranteed way to spread their genes. In the present, domesticated animals can be considered the most "successful" species, with 0 probability of becoming extinct, while many "free" wild animals starve and are less able to reproduce.
1.5.4.7.1. Con: A social contract implies a mutual benefit. The relationship between domesticated animals and their human captures is not a mutually beneficial relationship. Animals raised for slaughter are artificially selected for traits that humans value, which inhibit the animal's ability to survive in the wild. Each of our domesticated species suffers from various illnesses and conditions that are a direct result of captivity and human exploitation.
1.5.4.8. Pro: To not eat an animal who dedicated it's entire life so that it can be consumed is not only wasteful but disrespectful towards that animal's sacrifice to sustain us.
1.5.5. Con: Animals should have rights because they are sentient beings.
1.5.5.1. Con: Animals do not conceive of pain and death the same way humans do.
1.5.5.1.1. Pro: The experience of pain is confounded by the conscious knowledge that pain is being inflicted with intent. Without consciousness pain lacks the existential terror of deliberative motive and merely becomes an unpleasant sensation.
1.5.5.1.1.1. Con: As Kant would point: You cannot access the "being" of the things, so you cannot feel the pain of the other animal. You can only see the phenomena. Looking to the phenomena of a screaming dog, you can't tell if he is living "existential pain", but you can surely tell that he is having a bad time.
1.5.5.1.2. Con: In order to feel pain, one must have some kind of consciousness, because he knows how to distinguish himself from the world. Its like: "It's me who feel pain", and not this stone or this other dog. One must have some kind of conscience of his body in-the-world, in order to feel pain.
1.5.5.1.2.1. Pro: If an entity feels pain, then it necessarily has a sense of self, because it can distinguish itself from the world. Self-awareness is, really, necessary to have a conscious feeling of pain, so, something feels pain, then it must be self-aware in some way. The difference between the 'pain' state of mind in different animals cannot be proven, since all of them show us the same phenomena: if, e.g., the ache is in their feet, they scream, stop walking or start limping, etc.
1.5.5.1.3. Pro: Most animals do not have preferences about their own futures, like humans do. It is therefore not wrong to kill them.
1.5.5.1.3.1. Con: Even if it is not wrong to kill animals, traditional methods of farming cause them significant pain, and animals do clearly have preferences against pain.
1.5.5.1.4. Con: Animals will generally attempt to avoid pain \(and often become distressed when they believe it is impending, such as during veterinary visits\), suggesting that they do not wish to experience it.
1.5.5.2. Pro: Because animals feel, they should be treated according to [utilitarian ethics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Utilitarianism), which advocates maximising pleasure and minimising suffering for all sentient beings.
1.5.5.2.1. Con: The pleasure of a human is worth more than the life of an animal because it is scarcer. Thus, utilitarian ethics suggest that humans should keep eating meat.
1.5.5.2.1.1. Pro: From a human perspective it is easy to produce more animals but difficult to create pleasure. Comparing the two, this makes pleasure more valuable to humans than animal lives.
1.5.5.2.1.1.1. Con: Emotions and pleasures can be produced through consumation of arts and drugs.
1.5.5.2.1.1.1.1. Pro: And connections and interactions with other humans, and experiences and sensations.
1.5.5.2.1.1.1.1.1. Pro: Many experiences can non-fatally involve animals, and yet produce pleasure e.g. petting a pet.
1.5.5.2.1.1.2. Con: There are many other ways for humans to get joy that do not require the suffering of animals e.g. spending time with loved ones, participating in hobbies and sports, and eating other food they enjoy that does not include animal products. This means joy is not a good reason for eating meat.
1.5.5.2.1.2. Con: Billions of animals are brutalized and tortured on factory farms every year. Since the only pleasure humans get from eating meat is gustatory pleasure, it is doubtful that the pain and suffering that these animals experience can ever be justified according to utilitarian principles.
1.5.5.2.1.2.1. Con: There is [way more](http://foundational-research.org/the-importance-of-wild-animal-suffering/) animal suffering going on in the wild than on human-made farms. Effective utilitarianism would thus have to start at considerations about how to ease the lot of wild animals.
1.5.5.2.1.2.1.1. Con: Domestic vertebrates comprise a majority of the vertebrate biomass on Earth; humans and domestic vertebrates together comprise an overwhelming majority \(\>95%\). Humans therefore control a majority of vertebrate suffering.
1.5.5.2.1.2.1.2. Con: It is sensible and more feasible to start taking action in areas that we are directly involved with and responsible for. Unlike the suffering of farm animals, the [suffering](http://foundational-research.org/the-importance-of-wild-animal-suffering/) of wild animals is in large part not related to human actions, but to natural predators, diseases, harsh natural habitats, naturally short life-spans, more offspring than can be fed, etc.
1.5.5.2.1.2.2. Con: There is much more impotance to mild and moderate rates of meat comsumption than just gustatory pleasures, and, evolutionarily, such [benefits](http://time.com/4252373/meat-eating-veganism-evolution/) of less energy dispensed at feeding and more calory rich diets were important to the development of human brain size, which, in turn, originated many of our current pleasures.
1.5.5.2.1.2.2.1. Con: The fact that meat was historically important for our development does not imply that we should continue eating it now.
1.5.5.2.1.2.2.1.1. Con: The fact that meat was the most efficient route for the development of civilization and human biology \(as [we do not have the proper enzymes to digest cellulose](http://science.jrank.org/pages/1335/Cellulose-Cellulose-digestion.html)\) does indeed suggest it is best to keep eating meat in mild or moderate quantities. Not necessarily as much as we ingest right now, but it certainly is not wrong.
1.5.5.2.1.3. Con: It is not necessarily true that human pleasure is scarce. People find many ways to experience pleasure, through spending time with loved ones, participating in hobbies and watching media entertainment. There are likely also other foods humans can eat to experience pleasure without relying on meat.
1.5.5.2.1.3.1. Pro: The food most commonly, directly, and immediately associated with pleasure in mainstream society is chocolate - a processed plant food.
1.5.5.2.1.4. Con: -> See 1.1.4.6.1.
1.5.5.2.2. Con: Humans are capable of culture, innovation, and the sublimation of instinct in order to act in an ethical manner. Animals are not, thus they are unequal to humans on a moral level.
1.5.5.2.2.1. Con: Plenty of researchers would disagree and argue that moral and ethical behavior can be observed in animals, for example [Frans de Waal](http://books.wwnorton.com/books/the-bonobo-and-the-atheist/) as well as [Marc Bekoff and Jessice Pierce](http://press.uchicago.edu/ucp/books/book/chicago/W/bo6407651.html).
1.5.5.2.2.1.1. Pro: [Frans de Waal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frans_de_Waal) is an expert in both zoology and ethology. This makes him an ideal candidate to judge the ethical behavior observed in animals.
1.5.5.2.2.1.2. Pro: Dominique Lestel wrote a book called Les Origines Animales de la Culture, parting from Uexküll phenomenological reviews, that claims that other animals have different ways of life, cultures, and even aesthetical senses.
1.5.5.2.2.2. Con: Some humans are not capable of culture, innovation, or sublimation of instinct, and are thus not able to act in an ethical manner \(i.e cognitive disability\), but we still consider their interests morally relevant.
1.5.5.2.2.2.1. Con: The reason for that is that these people are our own kind.
1.5.5.2.2.3. Con: This does not excuse cruelty or unnecessary harm.
1.5.5.2.2.4. Con: For any supposed capacity that humans possess and animals lack, and in virtue of which humans but not animals are taken to have rights, some humans will lack that capacity and some animals may possess that capacity. For instance, humans generally have the ability to reason, while most animals lack this capacity. But this cannot be the basis on which humans have and animals lack rights, because certain humans, including young infants, lack the ability to reason and certain animals may have it.
1.5.5.2.2.5. Con: Animals are able to suppress their instincts, something that, for example, dog training aims at.
1.5.5.2.3. Pro: Act utilitarian [Peter Singer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animal_Liberation_%28book%29) argues that the Basic Principle of Equality should be extended to nonhuman animals and that it would demand us to equally consider the interests of animals.
1.5.5.2.3.1. Con: Singer bases his argument on the presence of interests. If a being has interests, these have to be taken into account. Yet this would make eating plants as immoral as eating animals as they obviously have interests, for example in survival and reproduction. Singer's argument therefore suffers [from arbitrary delineations](http://www.abc.net.au/religion/articles/2014/08/05/4061202.htm).
1.5.5.2.3.1.1. Con: -> See 1.1.4.8.1.1.6.1.3.
1.5.5.2.3.1.2. Con: -> See 1.1.4.8.1.1.6.1.4.
1.5.5.2.4. Pro: -> See 1.5.5.2.1.2.
1.5.5.2.5. Con: Utilitarian ethics is not only a disputed way of thinking, but is very difficult to implement \(for instance, what is the appropriate time span of pain/pleasure that we are computing?\), especially in nature, which does not care about utilitarianism.
1.5.5.2.6. Con: We are beginning to find evidence that [plants feel as well.](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=CrrSAc-vjG4) If plants and animals both feel, we cannot afford to treat them both with utilitarian ethics, we have to eat something! Animals are not more deserving of care than plants. No matter what we do, we'll be making an ethical compromise and killing a living, feeling being.
1.5.5.2.6.1. Con: Further in the future, it could be possible to transition to wholly synthetic food or other means of fuelling our bodies.
1.5.5.2.6.2. Pro: Native traditions simply seek to honor every part, by using every part as it is best and intended - recognizing and honoring the life and soul in all things.
1.5.5.2.6.3. Con: We have to make a choice. Because we must eat organically, we must eat plants. However, for plants, being consumed is part of their reproductive cycle. For example, they bear fruit which is mean to be consumed, thereby scattering their seeds.
1.5.5.2.7. Con: Many animals are incapable of feeling pain. Depending on the definition of sentient this may not be able to be applied to meat from any source in general.
1.5.5.2.7.1. Pro: A trivial example would be a jellyfish, which does not have a brain at all. [Most invertebrates](https://pdfs.semanticscholar.org/6b16/c458c4eec3cc163af5f68835ceea1a0f7a10.pdf) are likely incapable of feeling pain in the same way we do as well.
1.5.5.2.8. Pro: Farm [animals feel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animal_consciousness) pleasure and sadness, excitement and resentment, depression, fear and pain. They are [sentient](http://www.livescience.com/39481-time-to-declare-animal-sentience.html) and intelligent beings, thus utilitarianism can be applied to them. \([psychologytoday.com](https://www.psychologytoday.com/blog/animal-emotions/201306/universal-declaration-animal-sentience-no-pretending)\)
1.5.5.2.8.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.1.4.1.2.6.
1.5.5.2.8.2. Pro: Animals are now legally recognised as [sentient](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/world/australasia/animals-are-now-legally-recognised-as-sentient-beings-in-new-zealand-10256006.html) beings in New Zealand.
1.5.5.2.8.2.1. Con: Laws make for weak argument, as the ongoing debate on itself, could change said laws. Human law systems are different from natural laws.
1.5.5.2.8.3. Con: Animals' sentience is [instantaneous](https://news.nationalgeographic.com/2015/02/150225-dogs-memories-animals-chimpanzees-science-mind-psychology/) and ephemeral, they are not capable of long term predictions, therefore not suffering in anticipation to existential angst in events such as death.
1.5.5.2.8.3.1. Con: -> See 1.5.1.3.6.2.1.
1.5.5.2.9. Con: A pleasure maximizing ethical paradigm might just as easily justify breeding livestock for food, raising them in a way that maximizes their happiness, and killing them in the most humane manner possible. By bringing a life into this world you are increasing total happiness and satisfying the tastes/preferences of humans who desire meat as food.
1.5.5.2.10. Con: The argument has been made that plants are [equally sentient and intelligent](http://www.theguardian.com/environment/radical-conservation/2015/aug/04/plants-intelligent-sentient-book-brilliant-green-internet), even though not in the same sense as humans and animals are. By the same logic, utilitarian ethics would forbid the killing of plants.
1.5.5.2.10.1. Con: Eating crops is necessary for many people to survive. Eating meat is not. Thus, there is a difference in the logic applied.
1.5.5.2.10.1.1. Con: It is possible to only eat meat for one year. As a study from 1930 illustrates '[nothing much](https://swizec.com/blog/week-17-what-happens-when-you-only-eat-meat-for-a-year/swizec/6534)' happens.
1.5.5.2.10.1.2. Con: [Inuit](http://www.popsci.com/science/article/2011-04/fyi-what-would-happen-if-i-ate-nothing-meat) ate mainly meat and hardly any plants and survived.
1.5.5.2.10.1.2.1. Pro: They did not only survive but appear to be living pretty [healthy](http://www.theiflife.com/the-inuit-paradox-high-fat-lower-heart-disease-and-cancer/) lives.
1.5.5.2.10.1.3. Pro: Crops provide us with important [minerals](http://www.eatbalanced.com/why-eat-balanced/why-do-we-need-minerals/) that are essential for a variety of bodily functions.
1.5.5.2.10.1.3.1. Con: By eating meat, people can satisfy their [mineral](http://www.health-alternatives.com/beef-nutrients.html) demand as well.
1.5.5.2.10.1.4. Pro: Dietary [fibre](http://www.eatbalanced.com/why-eat-balanced/why-do-we-need-fibre/) is present in all plant foods – fruits, vegetables and grains – but not in animal foods. Without fibre we cannot survive and therefore need to eat plants.
1.5.5.2.10.1.4.1. Pro: Fibre smooths out digestion and absorption of glucose and fats in the small intestine.
