1.5.5.2.10.1.4.2. Pro: Fibre provides fuel for the healthful or “good” bacteria in our large intestine which in turn benefit us by making vitamin B12, and by releasing volatile fatty acids from the dietary fibre which are important for the health of our colon.
1.5.5.2.10.1.4.3. Pro: Fibre speeds up transit though the intestines to remove waste and toxins from our body.
1.5.5.2.10.1.5. Pro: Plants and crops are often the cheapest food that people can buy to sustain themselves, and if people lack resources this may mean it is very necessary for their survival.
1.5.5.2.10.2. Con: As noted in the linked article, plants are able to survive losing a large portion of their biomass - and many produce fruiting bodies deliberately intended for consumption by animals - whereas harvesting an animal for meat kills it 100% of the time. It is more possible to ethically harvest \(some\) plants than animals.
1.5.5.2.10.3. Con: Farming animals for meat requires either farming plants for their food, or allowing them to graze in deliberately altered semi-natural environments. Therefore, farming meat produces a greater utilitarian loss than farming plants, as farming plants only harms and kills plants directly, while farming meat harms and kills both plants and the animals.
1.5.5.3. Pro: Animals from a wide range of species have been shown to experience [emotions](https://www.psychologytoday.com/blog/animal-emotions/201306/universal-declaration-animal-sentience-no-pretending) including happiness, grief, empathy and jealousy.
1.5.5.3.1. Con: -> See 1.5.5.2.8.3.
1.5.5.4. Con: Sentience is not a sufficient condition for having rights. For example, ants are sentient, but it would be unreasonable to say that they have a right to life.
1.5.5.4.1. Con: Sentience is a matter of degree. Farm animals are sufficiently sentient that we should grant them rights.
1.5.5.4.2. Con: It would, however, be reasonable to say we should not interfere with them without reason to, thereby implying certain rights to "life" and non-interference.
1.5.5.5. Pro: We should grant rights to all sentient beings because there is no consistent way of differentiating between humans \(who have rights\) and other sentient beings.
1.5.5.5.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.4.8.1.1.1.3.
1.5.5.5.2. Con: Humans are participants in a human moral community; this is sufficient to distinguish them from animals.
1.5.5.5.3. Con: Since there is no way of knowing how truly 'sentient' or 'non-sentient' something is, drawing the line somewhere on that spectrum between what is and is not sentient would be far more arbitrary than if we just drew the line between human and non-human life.
1.5.5.5.4. Pro: The ability to paint some lives as inferior despite the fact that intelligence is relative also allows for human suffering, and assuring the basic protection of all conscious lives would be a step towards a more conscientious global community.
1.5.5.5.5. Pro: Humans understand that we feel pain and that other beings can feel pain, including animals. Although they may not understand our pain, we understand theirs, which gives our actions towards them moral weight. If killing is wrong than killing animals is wrong.
1.5.5.6. Con: Animals are sentient, but not rational, therefore cannot predict suffering or conceive their fates in slaughter houses. Rights emerge from the prediction of suffering.
1.5.5.6.1. Con: This implies unanticipated suffering is morally acceptable.
1.5.5.6.2. Con: [Animals demonstrate causal reasoning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Causal_reasoning#Causal_reasoning_in_non-human_animals) in the same way humans do. For example Pavlov's dogs demonstrate animals can learn about cause and effect, [some animals use causal reasoning to make tools](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tool_use_by_animals)and [crows can reason about hidden causal agents in a way which was incorrectly believed to be unique to humans.](http://www.pnas.org/content/109/40/16389)
1.5.5.6.3. Con: Animals [demonstrate grief](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animal_grief) which implies that they are able to reason about suffering.
1.5.5.6.4. Con: Animals will try to save themselves or other members of their group when they reason that their lives are in danger.  Therefore they can predict suffering.
1.5.5.6.5. Con: 'Rights' are a human-created concept with the dominant 'class or species' deciding the rules. Many ethnic minorities don't receive the same 'rights' \(treatment\) because of this; and we should consider animals as in a similarly marginalised position.
1.5.5.7. Con: Animals are not as self aware as humans, so they would not be aware of their coming death until it's already happened. Especially as the methods of doing so are quick and painless.
1.5.5.7.1. Pro: Example of a farm which uses painless methods: [youtu.be](https://youtu.be/id5bPjJG7IU?t=1m49s)
1.5.5.8. Pro: Non-human animals, just like humans, are sencient and have a conscience. The conscience issue has been stated by '[The Cambridge Declaration on Consciousness](http://fcmconference.org/img/CambridgeDeclarationOnConsciousness.pdf)' issued by a prominent international group of cognitive neuroscientists,
neuropharmacologists, neurophysiologists, neuroanatomists and computational neuroscientists gathered at The University of Cambridge.
1.5.5.9. Pro: If we accept the fact that killing humans is wrong, as we are sentient beings, so is the killing of animals wrong as well. It is a clear fact that animals feel pain, join, etc. Some of them live among us as fellows, helpers...so if that's the reality for some that should be for all of them. It would be fair that way.
1.5.6. Con: Eating meat violates animals' right to life.
1.5.6.1. Con: -> See 1.5.4.
1.5.6.2. Pro: Animals desire to continue existing. This is sufficient to grant them a right to life.
1.5.6.2.1. Con: Simply having a desire does not constitute having a fundamental right. Rights are constructed by humans to make sense of laws of nature that a living things needs in order to survive. Nature constantly uses death to create new life. It is a beautiful circle that we are a part of.
1.5.6.2.1.1. Con: The only reason animals have not human-given rights is because we have chosen not to give rights to them. By their own existence, they have the right to life.
1.5.6.2.1.1.1. Con: Rights are a social construct for regulating the behaviour of humans within a society, and only function because humans are capable of conceptualizing the ideas of rights and responsibilities. Therefore, the reason that animals have not been given human rights may be because they are not applicable to most animals by definition.
1.5.6.2.1.1.2. Con: Rights only exist in the minds of humans, so does the 'right to life'. Therefore, the claim contradicts itself.
1.5.6.2.2. Con: Such a right would be internally inconsistent. Prey animals would then have a right to life which if policed by humans would deny a right of life to their natural predators which would then starve.
1.5.6.2.2.1. Con: There is a difference between policing and moderating our own actions, and attempting to police the entire natural world by our standards.
1.5.6.2.3. Pro: Animals have emotions, social systems, instincts, and intelligence. Humans should respect their existence. We have evolved and no longer need to prey on animals for our existence.
1.5.6.3. Con: With that logic, eating plants, fruits or vegetables also violates their right to life. There is nothing wrong with the occurrence of natural selection and the circle of life.
1.5.6.3.1. Con: Fruiting plants' fruiting bodies are deliberately grown to be removed and eaten in order to spread the plant's seeds, and are not essential to its individual survival. Fruiting plants are easily capable of withstanding the loss of nutrients which go into producing fruit and continuing to thrive, while killing an animal for meat outright ends that animal's life permanently.
1.5.6.3.2. Con: There is no "natural selection" occurring on a farm in any case unless certain animals are deliberately spared the harvest for specific reasons, and/or deliberately used to breed - and even then, this is preferential breeding and not natural selection. Animals born and bred on farms are not naturally occurring specimens.
1.5.6.3.3. Con: "Their right to life" implies a personhood which isn't a characteristic the scientific community agrees plants have. The correct phrase is "its right to life", which plants don't have.
1.5.6.4. Con: Animals have no rights because they have no obligations.
1.5.6.4.1. Con: Animals are living beings with the right to not be inflicted with unnecessary suffering or unjustly killed outside of an extraordinary emergency.
1.5.6.4.2. Pro: Every right one possesses imposes a responsibility on all parties to respect it. As animals are incapable of following this responsibility, they are not offered them.
1.5.6.4.3. Con: Animal rights can correspond to human obligations, but not any obligations from other animals.
1.5.6.5. Con: Nature doesn't give animals a right to life. It kills them cruelly and in great numbers.
1.5.7. Con: Animals do in fact have rights. Many forms of cruelty are explicitly outlawed. One cannot, for instance, torture a dog for years in their basement. Therefore the statement that animals do not have rights is fundamentally false.
1.5.7.1. Con: Humans have legal \(and ethical\) obligations with respect to animals, but that doesn't mean animals have reciprocal rights \(they cannot sue, be awarded damages, etc.\). Our obligations to animals exist as a matter of appropriate human behavior, not something that belongs to the animal.
1.5.7.1.1. Con: Our current treatment of animals is not appropriate human behaviour \(factory farming, mass slaughter, etc.\).
1.5.7.1.1.1. Con: That can be true without supporting the proposition that eating meat is per se unethical
1.5.7.1.1.1.1. Con: Eating meat inevitably requires causing an animal's death \(unless meat is scavenged, which is not practical today\), and usually \(varying degrees of\) suffering beforehand. If one argues that appropriate human behaviour/ethics is/involves harm minimization, this is inappropriate and unethical human behaviour.
1.5.7.1.2. Con: Our obligations to other humans exist solely as a matter of appropriate human behavior as well. And an impaired human might also not be able to "sue" or handle "money," and yet they would \(or would not\) have rights just like animals would \(or would not\) -- with perfect symmetry. Any model of what "Rights" are supposed to be can be shown to apply simultaneously to all animals, not just the human animal.
1.5.7.1.2.1. Con: This isn't correct as a legal matter. An impaired human without the capacity to sue for themselves will have a guardian appointed to act on their behalf. No equivalent process exists for animals. As a legal matter, animals do not have rights.
1.5.7.1.2.1.1. Con: [This is conflation of law with morality.](https://reasonandmeaning.com/2016/03/31/the-difference-between-the-moral-and-the-legal/)
1.5.7.1.2.1.2. Con: Humans have taken it on themselves to sue for animal's rights.
1.5.7.2. Con: The original statement this is responding to was indicating that animals "lack rights".  That is not false.  We do not live in a completely free society, and there are rules and laws which permit and prohibit certain behaviors.  Animals are lacking certain rights- in that humans are given rights which animals are not, such as a dog cannot walk unleashed in public areas.  Contrarily people lack certain rights which animals have, such as dogs being able to defecate in public.
1.5.8. Pro: Rights by their nature correspond to duties. Since the actions of animals lack moral content they can't be said to have duties; we don't talk about a tiger or a cow being morally good or bad, or assign moral obligations to them, because by their nature they are not rational beings.
1.5.8.1. Con: By this reasoning, if humans have the right to raise animals, then we also have a corresponding duty of care towards those animals.
1.5.8.2. Con: The claim that animals are not rational beings is debated. This book gives an overview of the different studies on animal rationality and interpretations of the term rationality [philpapers.org](https://philpapers.org/rec/HURRA). Since it is not clear that animals are not rational beings, you cannot just assume their actions lack moral content. And even if their actions lacked moral content to them, this might not be the case for us, as human beings interpreting their behaviour.
1.5.8.3. Con: Humans should not predate other species for the sake of it, only as and when it's out of necessity.
1.5.8.4. Con: No one argued the moral onus to not eat meat was on the animals. We as humans have rights and therefore duties.
1.5.8.5. Con: The claim that animals don't have rights, because rights equate with duty is an outdated one made by Kant, Descartes, and others. We now have greater insight- and more questions- about animal intelligence and the nature of consciousness in general, so can no longer hold these claims as valid arguments.
1.5.8.6. Pro: People should treat things \(other humans, animals, plants, or minerals\) according to their natures. It is natural for a prey animal to be eaten, and so there is nothing immoral in eating it.
1.5.8.6.1. Con: No "prey" animal is eaten by its own nature, rather because other animals evolve to prey on it.
1.5.8.6.2. Con: Morality is not predicated upon the nature of or actions of others.
1.5.8.6.3. Con: Humans treat all sorts of things unnaturally: for example, using animal products to create glue, or minerals to create computers. Clearly humans have the capacity to go beyond a thing's "natural purpose."
1.5.8.6.4. Pro: All life suffers, and all life dies. Furthermore, all life inevitably results in the suffering and death of other life. Competition for food and habitat, predation, competition to reproduce...all of this happens even without human influence, even in microbial ecosystems. So the logical conclusion of this claim is that life itself is cruel and immoral. It is a spiritual, subjective, and therefore unsubstantiated claim. One cannot possibly know the truth.
1.5.8.6.4.1. Con: There was once a man who said that the mysteries of the human body were mysteries infinitely beyond the comprehension of science. Modern medicine would rather put an egg on his face. Any claim that certain knowledge is impossible to possess is one which should be viewed with scepticism, as there is no knowledge which is arbitrarily and absolutely incomprehensible.
1.5.8.6.4.1.1. Con: Unless you are familiar with the means by which human beings can flourish without creating suffering, or you can provide sources, this claim is irrelevant.
1.5.8.6.4.1.1.1. Con: The argument is that if we continue searching, we will - or may - eventually find.

More practically, there are uninhabited planets, and vast swathes of empty space, on and in which a sufficiently advanced humanity could live without disturbing any other living creature. And this is only one possibility of many.
1.5.8.6.4.2. Con: Suffering is unpleasant, and all sufficiently conscious animals attempt to avoid it. \(Even less conscious animals reflexively avoid pain!\) This suggests it is something we should not deliberately inflict, and take reasonable steps to avoid inflicting in others, as well as avoiding ourselves. Even if we cannot perfect the world, we should attempt to improve it.
1.5.8.6.4.2.1. Con: Yes. All conscious beings avoid suffering....to the detriment of other life. Everything any being does to alleviate its own suffering causes suffering in other beings.
1.5.8.6.4.2.1.1. Con: This is evidently not true. If I am in an uncomfortable physical position, for instance, I can change position to make myself more comfortable without affecting any other living thing.
1.5.8.6.4.3. Con: If life only produced suffering and negative experiences, this argument may hold water. However, life also produces joy and pleasure and enjoyable experiences. As such, according to [Utilitarianism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Utilitarianism) and similar ethical theories, our "job" or at least the most moral courses of action are to minimize suffering while maximizing pleasure and pleasant experiences.
1.5.8.6.4.3.1. Con: [Life creates both suffering and joy, but suffering is unavoidable.](https://www.kialo.com/eating-meat-is-wrong-1229/1229.0=1229.1+1229.21-1229.4083-1229.4289-1229.4314)
1.5.8.6.4.3.1.1. Con: You cannot simply link another \(unsupported\) claim as "evidence". Furthermore, even if this were 100% true, it would still be possible to attempt to create the most joy possible while creating the least suffering possible. Neither of these claims rebuts the ultimate point, which is that even if perfection is impossible, improvement is not.
1.5.8.6.4.4. Con: This claim is invoking moral skepticism - there is no objective basis for wrong and right. Holding this position is extremely impractical as it permits all human behaviour and it follows that actions such as murder and rape- both observable in non-human life- are acceptable. 

In reality, this is not the case. Human behaviour is governed by norms whose content is subjective but whose existence  is not. Subjective though it may be, we accept that there are principles of right and wrong.
1.5.8.6.4.4.1. Con: The objective basis for wrong and right is provided by the system of morality used in the situation. The claim assumes that there is an objective right and wrong, which is ex definitio contradictory.
1.5.8.6.4.4.2. Con: This claim inaccurately describes its parent. The parent claim is questioning the objectivity of ITS parent, and questioning the authority of the claimant to rule on the morality of participating in the circle of life by eating meat. The claimant argues that it is in fact cynical to pass judgment on the natural circle of life and those who participate in it. The claimant argues that it is impossible, and in fact immoral, for humans to pass judgment on life itself.
1.5.8.6.4.4.3. Con: The norm for human behavior is not to extend all human rights to all animal life.
1.5.8.7. Con: Rights correspond to duties that moral agents have towards the holder of rights, not necessarily duties that the holder of rights has.
1.5.8.8. Con: Rights are based on the ability to suffer, not on the ability to think rationally.
1.5.8.9. Con: By this logic we should be able to eat severely mentally disabled people or the insane, since many of them are incapable of acting with moral content \(which is why they often can not be held criminally liable for their actions\). Clearly that would be immoral. For the same reasons, this argument is flawed.
1.5.8.10. Con: The rights of animals can correspond to the duties of humans, but not to duties of other animals.
1.5.9. Con: Animals not having rights doesn't imply in any way that killing and torturing them for our consumption is moral.

1.5.9.2. Pro: That logic is used for torturing "terrorists". We created the Geneva convention in order to fight "respectfully". Animals deserve the same if we want to claim moral superiority.
1.5.9.3. Pro: No living being should be killed for food when there are other,widely accessible alternatives.
1.5.10. Pro: Animals lack free will.
1.5.10.1. Pro: As a human, we know that we make choices and we also see each other making choices. Animals, by contrast, mostly seem to be biotic machines, always trying to get the components they need to run, and spread.
1.5.10.1.1. Con: The same argument could be made of all observed human behaviours - we could be simply more complex biotic machines with more complex goals, and able to delude ourselves into thinking we have free will as we are driven to follow those goals.
1.5.10.2. Con: Many animals have demonstrated free will through play, associations and affections with humans and other animals, and more. Anyone who has owned a cat can attest that animals demonstrate free will; dolphins and primates also frequently exhibit it.
1.5.10.2.1. Con: The ability of something to exhibit complex behaviour does not imply that that thing has free will. For example, computer simulations can simulate playful behaviour, but they do not have free will.
1.5.10.3. Con: Because something lacks 'free will', does not mean it is incapable of suffering.
1.5.10.4. Con: Without evidence, it could just as easily be stated that humans also lack free will, leaving all factors equal on this standpoint.
1.5.10.5. Con: Assuming humans have free will, there's just as much reason to believe some animals do as well. Many of them have highly complex minds and problem-solving ability, crows being a good example. Contrary to popular belief, pigs are also highly intelligent.
1.5.10.6. Con: Speciesism is the ideology that one species is 'better than' another simply because they view their species specific abilities as inherently more valueable than that of another species. Many humans view cognition as the valuable human attribute that raises the value of our species above that of other animals, however, each animal contributes to the health and well-being of the planet in their own unique way, and it is impossible to categorize one characteristic as more valuable than the next.
1.5.10.7. Con: There is no "moral" way to kill an innocent animal who thinks, feels and wants to live — just like humans do.
1.5.10.7.1. Con: Animals are neither innocent nor guilty; these are human categories which cannot be applied to animals.

1.5.12. Pro: Animals are only given rights by us. If we were, as a society, to say that animals don’t have rights then they wouldn’t have rights. Ethics aren’t inherent for beings that aren’t capable of complex thought. A dog is only able to understand that the rumble in their stomach means they’re hungry — they can’t contemplate whether or not they truly exist or what it means to know something.
1.5.12.1. Con: The same arbitrary extension extends to human people. We extend rights to humans as long as they do not pose a threat to arbitrary legal standards. Much of this is fear-based and mistaken - decidedly amoral. Right and Wrong is a moral argument, not a legal argument.

1.5.13. Con: Rights are socially constructed by fallible and limited humans. Animals not having enforced 'rights' does not mean that it is okay to do anything to animals, only that the humans in a position of power creating rights did not think it worthwhile to give them rights.
1.5.13.1. Pro: Animals not having recognised legal rights does not mean that killing them is an acceptable or appropriate course of action.
1.5.13.1.1. Pro: When black people in America didn't have human rights it was not ethically correct for other people to kill them. The law isn't an arbiter of morality.
1.5.13.1.1.1. Con: People had no problem killing their slaves, as long as they were there's to kill. They were beaten and worked to death on the regular.

1.5.13.1.1.3. Pro: A lack of legal rights says nothing about innate rights.
1.5.13.1.2. Pro: What is permissible and what is advisable are entirely separate matters.

For example, rocks do not have feelings or rights, they fit in our mouths, and it is not presently illegal to eat them. None of these facts have a bearing on the question "should humans eat rocks?"
1.5.13.1.2.1. Con: It is not an inherent part of human nature and biology to eat rocks, making this analogy flawed.
1.5.13.1.2.1.1. Con: It is not an established intrinsic component of human nature or biology to eat meat either.
1.5.13.1.2.1.2. Con: This analogy is meant only to illustrate one distinction between what is advisable and what is permissible. Which is a distinct and separate claim, completely outside the purview of human nature or biology.
1.5.13.1.3. Pro: Morals are not the consequence of law. Law is derived from a pool of morals, and actions are derived from laws. In this way, laws do not represent morals effectively. Just because eating meat is legal and growing animals to be eaten is legal does not mean that eating meat is not morally wrong.

The planting of biome-appropriate, food-producing plants to alleviate hunger might also be considered.

1.8.9.8. Pro: "Extreme forms of suffering like starvation, dehydration, or being eaten alive by a predator are much more common in wild animals than farm animals. Crocodiles and hyenas disembowel their prey before killing them \(Tomasik 2009\). In birds, diseases like avian salmonellosis produce excruciating symptoms in the final days of life, such as depression, shivering, loss of appetite, and just before death, blindness, incoordination, staggering, tremor and convulsions." \([Journal of Practical Ethics](http://www.jpe.ox.ac.uk/papers/consistent-vegetarianism-and-the-suffering-of-wild-animals/)\)
1.8.9.9. Pro: From a utilitarian perspective, to take farming away likely puts those animals in a state of nature or non-existence. Given nature's cruelty, there's little reason to believe either are better for the animal.
1.8.9.10. Pro: Many animals are killed and consumed by predators every day. The only difference is that humans do not perceive sadness when those animals suffer and are killed in the wild. It is actually the individual's perception that an animal is suffering \(when a human butchers it\) that causes him/her to feel pity, and not the fact that animals are suffering \(which happens all the time, everywhere\).
1.8.9.10.1. Con: Similar remarks apply to the killing of humans by predators. Yet killing humans is clearly immoral.
1.8.9.10.1.1. Con: Humans killing humans is immoral, but predators killing humans is merely unfortunate as they don't have moral agency like we do. Since predators have no moral agency, killing them is not immoral. Since animals we use have no moral agency, killing them is not immoral.
1.8.9.10.2. Con: It's not about "perception" of animal suffering. The animals are suffering. The animals feel physical pain just as much as humans do. If a car ran over your dog you would empathize with your dog's physical pain. Cows, dogs and humans are all mammals and all feel pain. People just don't want to think about they pain that they are causing by paying farmers to kill animals.
1.8.9.10.2.1. Pro: Animals observably respond to pain and suffering - they flee when possible, and whimper or otherwise vocalise distress \(or respond with frenzied behaviour\) to sources of suffering which are not readily escapable.
1.8.9.11. Pro: Extreme forms of suffering like starvation, dehydration, or being eaten alive by a predator are much more common in wild animals than farm animals. If non-existence is a better alternative, we should be at least as concerned with animals under nature's "care"; potentially sterilizing or deliberately destroying wild animal habitats. \([See Journal of Practical Ethics](http://www.jpe.ox.ac.uk/papers/consistent-vegetarianism-and-the-suffering-of-wild-animals/)\)
1.8.10. Con: It may be natural for reflectively rational creatures like humans to become aware of the consequences of their actions and to abstain from doing things like eating meat.
1.8.10.1. Pro: In fact, since humans evolved as the result of natural processes, whatever humans do is, tautologically, natural.
1.8.10.2. Con: I'm having a hard time thinking of some case where I have to abandon part of my humanity to be moral. I don't think I can have a moral obligation to grow gills or wings.
1.8.11. Con: Humankind has exceeded 8 billion individuals in size. this achievement was possible due to unnatural mechanisms. Humankind has not followed the natural order \(at least\) since the industrial revolution. The concept of morality today is different from the concept of morality in pre-historic times.
1.8.11.1. Con: -> See 1.8.7.3.
1.8.12. Con: If predation is the natural order of the world, then humans should actually predate rather than blindly consuming meat from sources they are not familiar with. Humans desiring to eat meat should go into the wild, kill the wild animal, and then consume it. However, many people are more comfortable with blindly consuming animals whose death they do not have to consider — this is not the 'natural order' at all.
1.8.13. Con: Equating "natural" and "not immoral" clearly could be used to justify any activity and status quo, and is a fallacy. Eating meat must be judged not simply on whether it is "natural", but whether it is necessary \(in todays advanced society\), and in the context \(e.g. what is best for humans? for society? for the animals?\)
1.8.13.1. Con: Reducing our lives to just what is necessary would make it a sad time. Not even animals \(as opposed to humans\) reduce their living to what is necessary. We eat meat because we find pleasure in it.
1.8.14. Con: It may not be immoral to eat other animals, but the fact that having to grow animals to satisfy this need creates environmental issues may make it immoral.
1.8.14.1. Con: The argument could also be made about growing vegetables.  If we follow this rabbit hole then we would be scavenging natural resources to the point of greater harm.
1.8.15. Pro: If domesticated animals are raised ethically in a way as to have reasonable lives, then they are allowed to exist and experience life, where in a world without meat farming they probably would not.
1.8.15.1. Con: We \(humans\) shouldn't be the ones to choose what kind of lives animals should have and there is no guarantee that "ethically raised animals" have better lives than those who live in the wild.
1.8.15.2. Con: Only animals that have died of a natural cause can, therefore, be used as meat, since there is no such thing as killing ethically an animal that wants to live.
1.8.16. Con: This isn't just an issue about morality or natural order. This is also an issue about resources, raising animals, fishing and a lot of other related activities consume lots of resources. Population is growing and with the population, the needs of humanity as a whole. The massive amounts of resources needed for meat mass consuption in todays format is an ineficient and irresponsable use of the resources of the planet, which will unavoidibly affect humanity itself as well as the whole planet.
1.8.16.1. Pro: It could be regarded as immoral to use the resources of the planet in such an ineficcient and irresponsable way, considering that it might lead to hunger, as it dimishes availability of food for humanity as a whole, and the exhaustation of Earth's environment can as well be regarded as inmoral, as it affects all life on Earth.
1.8.17. Pro: Nature is much more cruel in the way of animal on animal violence. When humans kill most animals for food it is done in a fast and humane way.
1.8.17.1. Con: But the way we rear most animals is not so humane. And often the killing fails and is not so humane.
1.8.18. Con: Lots of animals are herbivores, and don't eat other animals. Eating animal is not more natural than eating plants.
1.8.18.1. Pro: Perhaps some animals had to resort to eating other living beings because of a lack of availability of nutritious, plant-based foods. Thus, carnivores, insectivores and omnivores developed. That doesn't mean that these eating styles are acceptable or moral when plant-based foods are nutritious and plentiful.
1.8.18.2. Con: There are examples of herbivores in nature, but those don't relate to humans; the omnivorous animals are what should be used to compare. Doing so, the conclusion is that, as with other omnivores, it is natural for humans to eat meat as a part of their diet.
1.9. Con: The harms of meat are not inherent to all forms of animal farming, and can be ameliorated through better consumer choice and/or moderation rather than complete abstention.
1.9.1. Pro: While much meat farming is cruel, it does not have to be. Ethical consumers could choose only to buy from ethical sources \(i.e. free range farming\) that afford animals the same \(if not better\) quality of life than they would have had in the wild, and a painless death.
1.9.1.1. Con: Every sentient animal has the same moral status. You therefore can't treat pigs and cows as means to an end if you can't treat humans as means to an end.
1.9.1.1.1. Con: We treat everybody who provides us with services \(e.g. plumbers\) as means to our ends.
1.9.1.1.1.1. Con: We don't treat their existences as means to our ends, but we enter into a contract \(which they freely and clearly accept\) with them for their services. Treating their existences as means to our ends would mean to enslave them.
1.9.1.1.2. Con: We should measure the morality of our actions towards animals on whether we fulfil their desires while they are alive, not on an abstract logical deduction regarding the nature of sentience. Pigs, for example, want to wallow in the mud, eat, reproduce, and have a painless death; we can fulfil all those desires and also eat the pigs afterwards, thus we can, morally, treat them as a means to an end.
1.9.1.1.2.1. Con: Pigs \(and most other animals\) only desire death when they are already in pain or distress, otherwise their actions in pursuing continued survival indicate they would prefer to live on indefinitely.
1.9.1.2. Pro: Eating meat is not cruel and immoral if the animals that are being consumed are provided with humane living conditions, able to live full lives, and die of natural causes.
1.9.1.2.1. Pro: Merely eating meat isn't cruel. What is cruel is the factory farm system we use to get the meat. If we can get the meat without causing undue pain to the animal, there is no issue.
1.9.1.2.1.1. Pro: The act of eating meat being immoral implies an accepted morality by all involved.  This is a [Naturalistic Fallacy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naturalistic_fallacy), a logical fallacy which is unsupportable.  Saying the eating of meat is immoral is to impose moral value 'good' upon a natural act, eating \(whatever it is\).  For instance is it really the act of eating meat that is being called immoral or is it the slaughtering practices?
1.9.1.2.1.2. Pro: Eating meat, in and of itself, cannot be cruel. [Cruel](http://www.dictionary.com/browse/cruel) is defined as willingly causing pain; unless you are eating the animal alive, it would be impossible to bring pain to a non-living piece of meat.
1.9.1.2.1.2.1. Con: If you are an ongoing meat eater, you are contributing to the demand for meat that causes animals to be slaughtered, and so in the long run you are willingly causing pain to animals, even if not at the exact stage that you eat them.
1.9.1.2.1.2.2. Con: If you eat an animal, you may not be directly inflicting pain upon it, but yours and the demand for meat among all consumers thereof, "caused" that death. If you and nobody else ate meat, there would be no money in farming it. Thus, meat eaters as a collective, do cause suffering and death, particularly with regards to factory farming.
1.9.1.2.1.3. Con: The meat must be sourced from somewhere. Even high welfare animal products and certifications involve some level of cruelty \(for example, gassing male chicks as opposed to liquidising them in the egg industry - better, but hardly cruelty free\). External monitoring of conditions can also be manipulated to put on a good show for the audits. By eating the meat, the consumer is complicit, just as using products made by slaves is tacitly supporting that industry.
1.9.1.2.2. Pro: Animals are currently being treated horribly, but many meat eaters are using their sway as actual consumers to improve animals' living conditions. This combined attention and sway is sure to help animal rights in the long run.
1.9.1.2.2.1. Pro: If we were to outlaw meat 10 years ago, all the animals in the meat making process would probably be slaughtered to cut losses. In light of recent changes, it is plausible that in 10 years' time farm animals will be allowed to live beyond the time when they are 'useful'.
1.9.1.2.2.2. Pro: [Free range eggs](https://www.economist.com/blogs/economist-explains/2016/12/economist-eggsplains) are taking over because they are more valuable then caged eggs.
1.9.1.2.2.3. Pro: The [organic beef market](https://agfundernews.com/grass-fed-beef-survey-story.html) is growing.
1.9.1.2.3. Pro: Cruelty, being a trait derived from the human mind, is only applicable to meat consumption if it is done irresponsibly as compared to other consumers of meat in the animal kingdom.
1.9.1.2.3.1. Con: It can be argued that with higher levels of intelligence and moral reasoning, humans should be held to a higher standard than other predatory animals.
1.9.1.2.3.1.1. Con: Possession of intelligence or moral reasoning do not imply the possession of the other.
1.9.1.2.3.1.1.1. Con: Humans generally have more of both than most other animals.
1.9.1.2.3.1.1.1.1. Con: Morality and behavior are subjective, and subsequently not quantifiable. Therefore a being cannot have more or less of any such quality.
1.9.1.2.3.1.1.1.1.1. Con: Humans have a greater ability to morally reason than, say, lions or crocodiles.
1.9.1.2.3.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con: Morality cannot be quantified.
1.9.1.2.3.1.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con: There are many moral systems which have the goal of doing just that.
1.9.1.2.3.1.1.1.2. Pro: Actually, I think you mean to say humans have a moral capacity, while animals don't, they just follow their instincts.
1.9.1.2.3.1.1.2. Pro: Defining ethics as the commonly accepted behavior or conduct of an intelligent being and defining morals as that which governs the definitions of right/good and wrong/evil, one should infer that the aforementioned are not synonymous. Therefore, no inference can be made for the possession of morals by an intelligent being or the possession of intelligence by a being of perceived morality.
1.9.1.2.4. Pro: Humans can make a choice to reduce the cruelty and immoral treatment of animals, as well as to reduce their consumption to a naturally sustainable amount.  This would be more likely to work if there weren't corporate "factories" for meat production--if humans were required to raise and/or hunt their own prey, and if there were distinct regulations disallowing and punishing animal cruelty on any spectrum.
1.9.1.2.5. Pro: Eating meat and cruel/immoral treatment of animals are not mutually exclusive - one can exist without the other. Large farms or "reserves" where animals are able to live a full and healthy life is a possible example. Likewise, it could also be assumed that ending the suffering for animals who are injured or already dying is more humane and less wasteful than allowing their suffering or wasting their remains.
1.9.1.2.6. Con: Even free range farming violates the rights of animals by imprisoning them \(albeit in a larger enclosure\) and eventually killing them against their will.
1.9.1.2.7. Con: Farm animals by definition cannot enjoy humane living conditions, since their freedom of movement will necessarily have to be violated.
1.9.1.2.7.1. Con: Following this line of logic, animals will be perpetually imprisoned because either shorelines will prevent them from "moving freely" to other continents or the earth's finite nature prevents them from "moving freely" to other planets.
1.9.1.3. Con: Animals cannot possibly enjoy the same quality of life that they'd have had in the wild if they were bred and raised with the sole purpose of being eaten.

1.9.1.3.3. Con: -> See 1.8.15.
1.9.1.3.4. Pro: The rearing of animals need not be seen as 'solely for eating', with additional responsibilities requiring a reasonable life. If meat consumption ceased, it is unlikely the farm animals would be reintroduced to the wild, they would simply vanish from existence.
1.9.1.3.5. Con: In the wild, there are no such concepts as compassion or malice. There is only nature, which is primal and brutal. A swift death at the hands of a hunter or farmer who will use the animal for food, clothing, etc. is a far better death than by natural predators, disease, or famine.
1.9.1.4. Con: The "free range" designation is not well regulated in the United States, and is more of a marketing term than a true indicator of how animals are treated. There are no rules enforcing the quality, size or degree of access to the outside.

1.9.1.6. Pro: -> See 1.5.4.
1.9.1.7. Con: Free range animals are often worse for the environment, [as they are more resource intensive in terms of land and less efficient in terms of food conversion](https://www.newscientist.com/article/2149220-grass-fed-beef-is-bad-for-the-planet-and-causes-climate-change/). Meanwhile, lower welfare animals benefit from efficiency of scale \(heat recapture in intensive farms for example\). Meat eaters must choose between welfare and the environment. Vegans need not make such a choice.
1.9.1.8. Pro: We can eat meat responsibly - choose carefully, develop small productions and local food, and make it a rare and savoured special dish.
1.9.1.8.1. Con: This still entails killing animals for their meat, and likely breeding and raising them in captivity.
1.9.1.8.2. Con: This would only work if all other meat production was stopped. Maybe stopping meat consumption in the present is the most effective way to get this to come about.
1.9.1.9. Pro: The best way to incentivise humane and quality meat production is to buy and eat more from local farms. Avoiding meat entirely causes industrial meat factories to cut costs even more, leading to even more unethical production. Vote with your money.
1.9.1.9.1. Con: If nobody eats meat, nobody will buy meat, and all meat producers will go out of business - including and especially the unethical ones.
1.9.1.9.2. Pro: If consumers stop eating meat altogether, then unethical meat producers know there is nothing they can do to get the demand back, so they do not change behaviour. If consumers switch to more ethical options, unethical producers are incentivised to produce more ethically.
1.9.1.10. Con: Do means justify ends? We cannot argue the humaneness of raising animals for consumption when the end goal is their slaughter en masse anyway.
1.9.1.10.1. Con: The humaneness of their treatment pre-slaughter is still relevant, as minimizing suffering is the end goal or one of the goals of many moral systems.
1.9.1.11. Pro: What is actually wrong is the treatment of animals in the  "meat industry", not the meat-eating itself. So, a change in the meat "production" would solve the problem, in a way that makes ceasing to eat meat unnecessary.
1.9.1.11.1. Con: Food production is fully integrated into capitalism, in which profits are always the priority. Unless/until that changes, this point is purely hypothetical/unrealistic.

1.9.1.16. Pro: Eating meat is not immoral, nor should it be excluded as a luxury; the production of meat can be cruel but the consumption is not.

1.9.2. Pro: Consumers can choose to purchase meat that is farmed in an environmentally friendly manner.

1.9.3. Con: Even under an 'ideal' farming circumstances \(i.e. free range; painless death\) killing an animal still violates its right to life. To do so for such as simple \(and substitutable\) need such as food is immoral.
1.9.3.1. Con: Most animals lack essential prerequisites for the right to life, like self-awareness or the ability to have desires about the future. While they may have a right to be free of suffering, they do not have a right to life.
1.9.3.1.1. Con: Claiming truth with no definition of the "right to life" or where this is a prerequisite to have desires about the future is not an argument. You have simply claimed these things as self evident truths. Also claimed animals are not self aware.  Which animals? Who said? When? etc.
1.9.3.1.2. Con: Animals demonstrate their desires for the future through their actions. For example, they try to flee from danger and become panicked in slaughterhouses, so they clearly have specific desires about the future to not be killed.
1.9.3.2. Pro: All animals have a right to live and be free of suffering.
1.9.3.2.1. Con: Some animals, who due to their reproduction disturb a natural balance, need to be killed in order to preserve biodiversity. 1.9.3.2.1.1. Con: Because biodiversity is greatest with immediate levels of stress some environmental interference is desired. The overpopulation of rabbits on [Norderney](http://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s11852-009-0056-9#/page-1), for example, has positive effects.
1.9.3.2.1.2. Con: Animals disturbing natural balance are generally the result of human actions \(e.g. resettlement\). It is not right to deny these animals their right to life, if it was indeed human actions that led to the disturbance of natural balance.
1.9.3.2.1.2.1. Con: Not killing the introduced rabbits will destroy the natural ecosystems of Norderney and ultimately lead to the death or depopulation of its native wildlife. Humans have a responsibility to fix the consequences of their actions; particularly when it prevents even greater harms from occurring.
1.9.3.2.1.2.2. Pro: -> See 1.2.1.4.1.1.9.1.

1.9.3.2.2. Con: Animals are not morally equivalent to humans and therefore do not possess an equal natural right to live.
1.9.3.2.2.1. Pro: All and only human beings have the capacity to make [free choices](http://www.animal-rights-library.com/texts-m/regan01.htm).
1.9.3.2.2.1.1. Con: It is highly disputable that babies and mentally disabled people have the capacity to make free choices.
1.9.3.2.2.1.1.1. Pro: Babies do not understand the dynamics of what things mean, what they are feeling nor what they are eating. These things mean nothing to them, they merely have an urge to eat/drink, and they will cry out to get the attention they require.
1.9.3.2.2.2. Pro: All and only human beings have a concept of "[self](http://www.animal-rights-library.com/texts-m/regan01.htm)". This makes humans [morally](https://www.jstor.org/stable/2669234?seq=1#page_scan_tab_contents) superior to animals.
1.9.3.2.2.2.1. Con: [Monkeys](http://link.springer.com/article/10.1007%2FBF02381922), [elephants](http://www.pnas.org/content/103/45/17053.abstract), [dolphins](http://www.pnas.org/content/98/10/5937.full) and [magpies](http://journals.plos.org/plosbiology/article?id=10.1371/journal.pbio.0060202) have been studied and were found to have self-awareness.

Con: The claim that the life of every human is equally worthwhile, [judged intrinsically](http://www.animal-rights-library.com/texts-m/regan01.pdf), cannot be construed as a basis for saying that each and every human being has an equal right to life. For the two claims would mean the same thing.
1.9.3.2.2.6. Pro: All and only human beings have various [positive interests](http://www.animal-rights-library.com/texts-m/regan01.pdf), such as desires, goals, hopes and preferences. This makes them morally superior to animals.

1.9.3.2.2.6.3.2. Con: Both humans and animals display both reflex and planned actions.

1.9.3.2.2.8. Con: Animals are a naturally occurring lifeform just as much as humans are.
1.9.3.2.3. Con: Most animals whose meat is eaten have been the property of humans. Their property rights conflict with animal rights.
1.9.3.2.3.1. Pro: That human rights outweigh animal rights is apparent in many legal circumstances.
1.9.3.2.3.1.1. Pro: It is legal to shoot a gorilla when there is the potential, yet uncertain, [danger](http://edition.cnn.com/2016/05/30/us/gorilla-shot-harambe/) that a human will be harmed. This illustrates that human rights are more important than animal rights.
1.9.3.2.3.1.1.1. Con: Human rights outweighing animal rights in situations where there is a choice between risking \(or taking\) a human life or an animal life is not the same as knowingly and deliberately mass factory-farming animals \(under often cruel conditions\) specifically for the consumption of humans.
1.9.3.2.3.1.2. Pro: A driver is prohibited to make an [emergency brake](http://www.theinjurylawyers.co.uk/injury-lawyers-blog/2013/03/19/rules-for-emergency-stop-for-an-animal/) for a cat when there is the danger of a collision with the car behind. Here human considerations weigh heavier than animal rights.
1.9.3.2.3.1.2.1. Con: -> See 1.9.3.2.3.1.1.1.
1.9.3.2.3.1.3. Con: That humans have created laws that place their rights above those of animals does not mean it is the correct ethical decision, it only shows that humans are interested in looking out for themselves.
1.9.3.2.3.1.4. Con: -> See 1.9.3.2.3.1.1.1.
1.9.3.2.3.2. Pro: Humans are [responsible](http://www.freewayinsurance.com/blog/auto-insurance/if-you-crash-into-a-cow-whos-liable-for-all-the-damage/) for damage caused by or danger done to 'their' animals, like cattle. This legal obligation results out of humans' property rights over these animals.
1.9.3.2.3.3. Pro: Nonhuman animals are the personal property of their owners, even if recognized as a special kind of property. As such, they cannot themselves possess legal rights, because they are the objects of the [exercise](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animals,_Property,_and_the_Law) of someone else's rights.
1.9.3.2.3.4. Pro: Animals are legally [classified](https://www.animallaw.info/article/animals-property) as 'goods' rather than having 'personality'.
1.9.3.2.3.4.1. Con: -> See 1.5.13.1.1.2.
1.9.3.2.3.5. Con: The right to property is not a natural right but a socially-enforced construct.
1.9.3.2.3.5.1. Con: It could equally be argued that so is the right to life.
1.9.3.2.3.6. Con: The property rights of slaveowners to their slaves should not interfere with the rights of the slaves. Similarly, the property rights of owners of animals should not interfere with the rights of the animals.
1.9.3.2.4. Pro: Humans currently give different consideration to animals such as pets \(whom we care for\) and farm animals \(whom we eat\). This is an arbitrary line to draw, as both have the same right to live.
1.9.3.2.4.1. Pro: There is significant evidence that[pigs are as smart and sociable as dogs](http://www.stuff.co.nz/science/8980423/Pigs-have-the-smarts-over-dogs), but because people enjoy eating their meat we choose to treat them as farm animals instead of potential pets.
1.9.3.2.4.2. Con: Giving different considerations to animals such as is it socially acceptable to eat it or not does not mean animals do or should have a right to life, natural or legal.
1.9.3.2.4.3. Con: The line that seems to be drawn is not by everyone, people have been eating meat ever since the very beginning and from all livestock possible. This idea for some is hard to access but yes people do also eat animals you keep as pets.
1.9.3.2.4.3.1. Pro: The line that is traced is different according to the different cultural contexts. But still, the claim is that the way that line is traced is arbitrary, whether the context you choose to analyse.
1.9.3.2.4.4. Pro: We place [undue importance on animals who are viewed as pets.](https://opinionator.blogs.nytimes.com/2011/03/15/some-animals-are-more-equal-than-others/?_r=0) If a pig was kept as a pet, one could be reported for animal cruelty for purposefully hurting it. However, pigs who are kept on farms for the purpose of providing meat are able to be treated very cruelly, showing the arbitrariness.
1.9.3.2.4.5. Con: The differentiation is not arbitrary but based on a history of social and emotional relationships which humans have with animals they generally do not eat, such as dogs and horses, but not with those they generally eat, such as cows and pigs.
1.9.3.2.5. Con: -> See 1.1.4.8.1.1.1.1.
1.9.3.2.6. Con: The limited sentience of most animal species means that they can be used to fulfill human desires, such as through farming, as long as it is not unnecessarily cruel.
1.9.3.2.6.1. Con: All animals display the instinct to self-preservation, that is, the instinct to avoid pain or damage to their bodies.
1.9.3.2.6.2. Con: We are not sure of this "limited sentience" and, in fact, the further into animal intelligence we dive, the more evidence we find that intelligence and self-awareness are far more widespread than we once thought. Some of the animals we consume as food are, in fact, quite intelligent - capable of problem solving, complex social relationships, even communicating in ways that border on language.
1.9.3.2.7. Pro: -> See 1.5.5.2.
1.9.3.2.8. Con: Animals do not have "rights" at all. They live in a state of eat or be eaten. Rights do not exist in the natural world at all, only in the socially constructed world that humans have created for themselves.
1.9.3.2.8.1. Pro: Fairness, being a construct of the human mind, is the concept which allows the intuition of rights. Animals cannot have rights, if their behavior does not acknowledge this assumed virtue, but rather could only be benefactors of human charity. Human charity would bestow, upon animals, privileges as opposed to rights.
1.9.3.2.8.2. Con: Even if society did not exist and humans lived in a state of anarchy, it would still be immoral to murder or torture people. This indicates that rights exist independently of social construction.
1.9.3.2.8.2.1. Con: While morality exists independently of social construction \(i.e. moral relativism is not logical\), it does not exist outside of human perspective, or at least not in the same way. Perspectivalism shows us that all of our facts about morality \(eg. murder is wrong\) comes exclusively from a human perspective. For instance, our closest cousins, Chimps, may injure or murder their own species in dominance disputes which results in the winner getting a chance to mate \(reward, not punishment\).
1.9.3.2.8.3. Con: This claim makes multiple statements. "Rights do not exist in the natural world" is true. However, "animals do not have rights at all" is false. They have rights when we grant rights to them.
1.9.3.2.8.3.1. Pro: There are many laws and moral codes which provide for the rights of animals.
1.9.3.2.9. Con: "Human rights" are not laws of the universe but rather a practical tool humans use to govern interaction with each other. They are a means of outlining and governing acceptable behavior so that we may live peacefully with each other. We can do this because we can communicate with each other and hold each other accountable. We cannot communicate with other species and hold them accountable in the same way yet.  A wolf does not understand, and will not respect, a universal right to life.
1.9.3.2.9.1. Con: This argument suggests that humans only have a right to life because a human society claims such. In other words, the right to life — for anything, is not an intrinsic value. Therefore, outside the confines of a society which grants \(which allows\) the right to life, humans do not have a right to life.
1.9.3.2.9.1.1. Pro: Suggesting that humans have this right to life regardless of one's current capacity to reason is due to humans' "intrinsic potential for reason" is not sufficient enough to justify our behavior in the way we separate ourselves from non-human animals. Dolphins, elephants, chimps, and many other non-human animals have continually demonstrated their capacity to reason \(and compassion toward other species\) and yet we don't grant them the right to life.
1.9.3.2.9.2. Con: Stating that one's capacity to understand, communicate with others, and hold others accountable for their actions is not sufficient reason to argue that non-human animals should not also have a right to life. Babies and the elderly who've lost their capacity for reason are two examples of humans who do not have the capacity to understand, communicate with others, and hold others accountable for their actions. Yet, our society grants them the right to life.
1.9.3.2.9.2.1. Con: Babies have the opportunity for free will and grown up human concioussness in the future. The animals do not.
1.9.3.2.9.2.1.1. Con: -> See 1.5.10.4.
1.9.3.2.9.3. Con: Rights can be enforced on both humans and nonhuman animals, such as by imprisoning a murderer \(or predator\) that would infringe on others' right to life and safety.
1.9.3.2.9.4. Con: We only experience human consciousness, but we don't know for sure whether animals are conscious or not. We do have some evidence suggesting that animals do have social systems which govern acceptable behaviour, communicate with each other, and hold each other accountable. We cannot discount their perspectives and values simply because we aren't able to understand them.
1.9.3.2.10. Con: Even if animals did live free of human slaughter, they would most likely die due to consumption by predators anyways so you might as well use the animals in a way that benefits humans.
1.9.3.2.10.1. Con: The way an animal lives is at least as important as how it dies, and farm animals have demonstrably lower quality of life than wild animals in at least some cases \(over-crowded cages/enclosures, absence of freedom of movement, inability to escape rivals, etc.\).
1.9.3.2.10.2. Con: The argument that you should do something to your own benefit because the same thing would happen if you didn't so you might as well is immoral. For example, I might as well rape that passed out girl because it's going to happen to her anyway so I might as well get to do it is an immoral way to approach something.
1.9.3.2.11. Con: It would be good to eradicate certain Mosquitos and parasites, so they cannot have such a right.
1.9.3.2.11.1. Con: This is already a debateable point, but even if we assume it's true, then it would be good to eradicate them because they do more harm \(and cost more lives\) than their own life's worth, NOT because their life has NO value of its own.
1.9.3.2.12. Con: A chicken or cow doesn't have any frame of reference, and hence doesn't know what a good or bad life is. We like to attribute meaning to our own lifes and subsequently, animals lives too. If a chicken does not know what it is like to live in the wilderness then is it suffering inside?
1.9.3.2.12.1. Con: If this argument was true, it would be moral for someone to be raised their whole life in something akin to a prison, as long as it was all they were exposed to, as they would not know there is a better life, but this is clearly false.
1.9.3.2.12.2. Con: Cows and chickens know what it is like to feel pain, and desire not to feel pain. This is sufficient to give them a right to live free of suffering.
1.9.3.2.12.3. Con: It doesn't matter what the chicken's frame of reference is, as long as the chicken is suffering under our treatment. In the words of the Utilitarian philosopher [Jeremy Bentham](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jeremy_Bentham), "the question is not, Can they reason? nor Can they talk? but, Can they suffer?"
1.9.3.2.13. Pro: -> See 1.1.4.8.1.1.1.
1.9.3.2.14. Pro: Imagine we could harvest meat from a meat plant. If you believe it is more ethical to harvest meat from a plant than from an animal, then you recognize that harvesting meat from animals is unethical.
1.9.3.2.14.1. Con: It is more ethical to give to charity than to not give to charity, but to not give to charity is not immoral.
1.9.3.2.14.1.1. Con: Not donating to charity does not harm anyone, whereas eating meat does harm. Not giving to charity is a morally neutral action. Eating meat is a morally negative action.
1.9.3.2.14.1.2. Con: Some forms of moral reasoning do in fact consider inaction immoral.
1.9.3.2.14.2. Con: There are other reasons why harvesting meat from a meat plant is more desirable. It is a more efficient process to grow a plant than to raise an animal. Therefore from a carbon standpoint the plant is more ethical.
1.9.3.2.15. Pro: It is our moral duty to minimize the suffering we cause to conscious beings. To the degree to which a being has the capacity to suffer, we have a responsibility to minimize the suffering we cause it.
1.9.3.2.16. Con: This statement is illogically selective. Why it should apply to just animals versus all living things? Why do all plants or microbes not have a right to live and be free of suffering?
1.9.3.2.16.1. Con: There is less clear and abundant evidence that plants and microorganisms suffer in ways analogous to humans and other animals.
1.9.3.2.17. Con: Animals bred for meat would not exist if their meat was not to be consumed. Wherein does their right to life reside if nobody is willing to create the circumstances of their existence?
1.9.3.2.17.1. Pro: Where would the animals go? If we stop farming animals for food, there will be numerous domesticated species that are no longer needed in farming but may also struggle to exist in the wild. If we could not agree space for them and invest in managing their transition to a stable wild ecosystem \(the path for which is uncertain and could take generations\), then the only alternative would be mass culling and ultimately the extinction of these species.
1.9.3.2.17.2. Pro: Countless animals have experienced life because they were bred as food. For me the better question is whether they are living a life worth living. Do they have room to roam, do they live without undue suffering, are they treated with respect while they are alive.
1.9.3.2.18. Pro: -> See 1.1.4.8.1.1.
1.9.3.2.19. Pro: Animals are sentient beings - they feel pain and have complex emotional lives. Many have family structures and are able to bond. Killing them for our pleasure when alternatives exist is immoral and archaic.
1.9.3.2.20. Pro: Meat comes from a living source that only dies in order for us to sustain our own living. We say it is morally incorrect to kill another human being but for an animal it is okay? This belief is hypocritical in nature.
1.9.3.2.20.1. Con: It is morally permissible to kill or cause the death of another human to save your own life in some situations.
1.9.3.2.20.2. Con: Sentence "killing humans is wrong" doesn't imply sentence "killing animals is wrong". Hence one person can simultaneously assert that killing humans is wrong, and that killing animals is okay, avoiding any contradiction or hypocrisy.
1.9.3.2.20.2.1. Pro: The word "animal" has wider range than word "human". Although every human is an animal, not every animal is a human. Statements concerning more narrow terms do not necessarily apply to wider terms.
1.9.3.2.21. Con: Without the raising of animals for slaughter, they wouldn't have had a life in the first place. They are fed and raised to maturity.
1.9.3.2.21.1. Con: They also wouldn't have been forced to suffer as they often are in modern factory farming.
1.9.3.2.22. Pro: Animals feel pain. And if you suggest that they are inferior therefore you have the right to do anything to them, you should also have the right to do anything you want with mentally challenged people or any other human who is inferior to you. Society is better than in the past exactly because of empathy from which morality arises. It is time to get past our arrogance towards other animals.
1.9.3.2.22.1. Con: Suggesting one human is \(inherently\) inferior to another is highly subjective and potentially problematic.
1.9.3.2.23. Pro: 70 billion animals [are sacrificed every year for consumption](https://www.ciwf.org.uk/media/3640540/ciwf_strategic_plan_20132017.pdf).
1.9.3.2.24. Pro: Ethics persuade us that we have a duty not to exploit anyone, and beyond that, a duty to actively help all victims of exploitation from their plight to the best of our ability. Animals in this case are innocent victims of mass exploitation.
1.9.3.2.24.1. Con: Animals are not innocent in the sense that they have no conflict of conscience over consuming their prey.
1.9.3.2.24.2. Con: A "duty to help" would need to include exploitation of nature as well as mankind. Nature's treatment of animals is as bad or worse than that of humans.
1.9.3.2.25. Con: The type of intensive agriculture required to produce vegetarian food on a scale to feed current populations inherently causes the death of animals. To plant acres of soy one must displace every single animal living there, eradicate every bird, mouse, squirrel that would eat the crop, poison every insect, grind up every creature in the path of the harvester, etc. Farming is an ecological disaster that represents death to every living being that is not the crop.
1.9.3.3. Pro: Eating 'happy meat' \(i.e. produced with less suffering\) is still discrimination, because we would not eat happy meat from e.g. humans or dogs. Killing a happy person \(e.g. a pig or human\) in order to eat him or her, remains a violation of the right not to be used as a means against one's will.
1.9.4. Con: In many, if not most, places it is much more difficult or [expensive](https://www.reuters.com/article/us-money-chicken-organic/is-organic-chicken-worth-the-price-idUSKBN0FM24Q20140717) for consumers to purchase truly 'ethical' meat rather than to simply opt for a vegetarian option.
1.9.4.1. Con: In those cases, consumers could have a moral obligation to either purchase the expensive ethical meat or not purchase meat at all. This still would not imply that we should never eat meat.
1.9.4.2. Pro: Nearly every piece of meat comes from factory farms with unethical conditions – the meat in the supermarket, in restaurants, public events et cetera. Choosing to abstain from this kind of meat means practically to completely abstain from meat as there is in fact no supply for it.
1.9.5. Con: The practice and habit of moderation is much more difficult to follow successfully than simple abstention.
1.9.5.1. Pro: As long as meat eaters retain a taste for and willingness to consume meat, there is the danger that they will end up purchasing unethically produced meat.
1.9.5.2. Con: Same is true for alcohol, still most people are not alcoholics and alcohol is not banned in most countries.
1.9.5.3. Con: Moderation being tougher than abstention does not imply that the latter is unfeasible.
1.9.5.4. Con: Moderation stems from discipline, which can be trained.
1.9.6. Con: The need for moderation recognises that the harms of meat exist. However if a harm is real and we as consumers have the power to prevent it at little personal cost, our moral duty is not to prevent it sometimes but rather to completely prevent it.
1.9.6.1. Con: The need for moderation does not mean the need to completely prevent it, as everything needs moderation. Even inherently good actions as exercising and the consumption of water.
1.9.6.1.1. Con: The argument is that the need for moderation is a _recogition_ of the harmful effects of meat consumption, not that most things are good in moderation. Your examples are of inherently good actions, which presumably are have no harmful consequences in moderation. There is arguably no lower limit where meat eating have 
no harmful consequences \(for the animals obviously\), so the argument is that if we can prevent it completely, at a little personal cost, we ought to.
1.9.6.2. Pro: The ethics of mass industrial-scale slaughter is abhorrent and is an undeniable indicator of mankind's ecocidal trajectory.  Only when we have developed the discipline necessary to stop eating meat will we be able to feel that our species has the right, the maturity and the characteristics necessary to assure our longevity. Until then we're just another bloodthirsty carnivore waiting for our own extinction event.
1.9.6.2.1. Con: All carnivores do not become extinct. The discipline to refine the ethics of the meat industry would be enough to make me feel that humanity has the right to longevity. Why would only the discipline to stop eating meat altogether be enough? Industrial-scale slaughter is only bad if it causes the animals physical or emotional pain, and the idea that slaughter and pain are necessarily connected is unimaginative, primal and regressive.
1.9.7. Pro: "Animals" is an overly broad term which includes creatures with a high range of mental capacity/complexity. Banning the consumption of lower orders of animals is not necessarily the same as banning the consumption of high level mammals.
1.9.7.1. Pro: For example, there is substantial evidence that insects [cannot feel pain](https://www.forbes.com/sites/quora/2016/07/12/are-insects-capable-of-feeling-pain/#b8ca415f20dc), so eating insect meat would not be immoral.
1.9.7.1.1. Con: American culture might not easily adapt insects. They believe insects are pests to avoid rather than go to to eat. This is evidenced by the pesticide market being bigger than the insect-eating market. Although, getting rid of the "pests" by eating them might solve the "pest" problem to begin with.
1.9.7.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.2.1.4.1.1.1.6.
1.9.7.1.3. Con: Just because insects cannot "sense" damage, does not mean we have the right to end their lives or severely impair them.
1.9.7.1.3.1. Pro: For example, if we eat a part of their body \(such as legs or wings\), but let them stay alive and let them live their life, their livelihood is debilitated. This reduces their chance of survival.
1.9.7.1.3.2. Pro: Just because insects cannot feel pain, does not mean we should eat them. They have an important role in the ecosystem and some species are highly protected due to their endangered status. Eating them could put both of these statuses at risk.
1.9.7.1.3.3. Pro: Even though they cannot feel pain, as the article mentions, they can sense damage. It is immoral to put animals through that negative sensation.
1.9.7.1.3.3.1. Con: Insects can "sense" damage in that they can respond to it, but they do not feel anything. Similarly, a crude robot might be able to respond to damage, but because it cannot feel the damage, it is not morally considerable.
1.9.7.1.3.3.1.1. Con: Insects are biological creatures that are living and are not robots. They should be treated as such. Just because robots act like insects does not mean they are the same thing \(correlation does not equal causation\)
1.9.7.1.4. Con: There is a possibility that insects feel pain in a way that humans do not fully understand yet. People in the past thought animals did not feel pain but yet reacted to it \(in a different manner\) is the level we are at right now. We should not take a chance \(of creating pain\) in case we are wrong but instead err on the side of caution.
1.9.7.1.4.1. Pro: Insects go through what looks like emotions similar to people. If an ant gets bothered enough, it seems to get angry because it moves more sporadically. When it is less threatened, it seems to calm down. If a bee feels threatened, it'll react out of what looks like fear for their life. These reactions are hard to dismiss when thinking about them having emotions.
1.9.7.1.5. Con: [There is a lot of new evidence that insects do have emotions. In fact, they are very similar to humans.](https://www.bustle.com/articles/187564-do-insects-have-emotions-the-answer-might-make-you-think-twice-before-swatting-one)
1.9.7.1.5.1. Pro: [Bees get stressed out, just like humans](https://phys.org/news/2011-06-stressed-bees-glass.html).
1.9.7.1.6. Pro: Eating insects is the future of meat consumption — it is easy to breed them in your own home.
1.9.7.2. Pro: We should learn to eat more insects which are more ecologically friendly. There's also a lot more variety and some cultures have been doing it for millennia.
1.9.7.2.1. Pro: Crickets are small enough to [grow in a box.](https://www.livinfarms.com/) A box uses much less land than livestock \(which use [30% of ice-free land worldwide](https://freshwaternz.files.wordpress.com/2016/03/livestock-production-inefficiencies.jpg?w=736)\).
1.9.7.2.2. Con: -> See 1.9.7.1.1.

1.9.7.3. Con: This argument assumes that sentience is the only objection to the consumption of animal products, failing to address the issues involved with the disruption of healthy ecosystems due to the large, growing human population.

1.9.9.1. Pro: Humans should stop eating animal meat that is farm produced. Period! Rear your own chicken, sheep, goat, pig, cow, duck. Have the gall to kill the very same animals you reared yourself, by all means, go ahead & eat meat.
1.9.9.1.1. Con: This is unfeasible given that most people live in cities and with city populations continuing to rise. [2 billion more people will live in cities by 2035](https://www.theguardian.com/cities/2016/oct/19/two-billion-more-people-live-cities-alejandro-aravena-habitat-3).

1.12.4. Pro: For most people, eating meat is needless and the needless killing of beings with a will to live is immoral.
1.12.4.1. Con: Those people don't have to eat it. No one is forcing anyone to eat meat, so there is no need to tell people who enjoy it to stop. There are plenty of other arguments in this thread related to the morals of the animal kingdom.
1.12.4.2. Con: Why is it immoral? Would you say it is also immoral for the average person to kill a spider in their house? They don't NEED to kill the spider most of the time.
1.12.4.2.1. Con: There are, in fact, better \(and more natural\) ways to manage pests than indiscriminately killing with pesticides. Naturally derived repellents are available and viable.
1.12.4.3. Pro: Causing unnecessary harm should be avoided on ethical grounds. Eating meat clearly does cause unnecessary harm, unless our survival depends upon it. Since humans can thrive without eating meat, it is an unethical thing to do.
1.12.4.4. Pro: Animals' lives are wasted when their meat is not eaten \(due to spoiling...\), causing them to suffer needlessly and thus die in vain.
1.12.4.4.1. Con: Vegetarian diets are also not free from wasted lives.[Jain vegetarians place value on plant lives](http://www.jainfoodie.com/jain-food-restrictions/). If a root vegetable goes to waste, they would believe that that plant died in vain.
1.12.4.4.2. Pro: Most meat comes from a killed animal, whereas not all vegetarian foods come from a killed animal or plant \(such as fruit\). Thus, a vegetarian diet could be cruelty-free for wasted food.
1.12.4.4.3. Pro: This is bad, because it accomplishes little and instead makes the world worse off than before.
